{"uuid":"c7549d3a-57fd-11e7-80b6-9bfa4c1f83d2","content":{"accessLevel":"subscribed","alternativeStandfirsts":{"promotionalStandfirst":"Agitators are far from perfect, but they can play a key role in spurring long-term thinking"},"alternativeTitles":{"contentPackageTitle":null,"promotionalTitle":"In praise of activist investors"},"body":"\u003cbody\u003e\u003ccontent data-embedded=\"true\" id=\"93a1ebd2-5807-11e7-1ed0-0c60966b289b\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/ImageSet\"\u003e\u003c/content\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis month the Dutch government debated a proposal to suspend all shareholder rights for a year in the event of an unsolicited takeover bid. This move, which we view as a “backwards step”, is the latest in a salvo of proposals to curb engaged investors. But it is also part of a broader debate about the perceived short-termism in markets, the weaknesses in corporate governance and the role of \u003ca href=\"https://www.ft.com/topics/themes/Investor_activism\"\u003eactivists\u003c/a\u003e which policymakers, boardrooms and investors wrestle with. That’s why we organised a summit this week to debate the merits and demerits of taking a long-term perspective and market-based solutions. I for one will be looking to praise, not bury, activist investors. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe history of short-termism is a long one. Keynes in the 1930s called short-termism a “contemporary evil”. From the pioneering “proxyteers” of the 1950s through the Barbarians at the Gate in the 1980s, to today’s activists, companies have often bemoaned engaged shareholders who have agitated for change. It remains true today.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eHowever, bosses perceive that short-termism is more acute than ever. A recent study commissioned by think-tank Focusing Capital on the Long Term suggested 87 per cent of executives felt pressure to deliver results within two years or less. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe pressure on executive teams is indeed high. But in our experience it is the disruptive impact of technology and its intersection with the big macro challenges of globalisation, political uncertainty and the scars of the financial crisis, which are often the root cause.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe second concern is that a large proportion of investors are focused on short-term returns. But it is hard to find systematic evidence of short-termism in markets. While plenty of investors are looking to trade small movements or focus on merger arbitrage, the vast weight of money is looking for long-term value. The valuations of tech giants or biotech stocks affirms that markets do value long-term earnings power and try to value the optionality inherent in such innovative sectors. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe next complaint is that companies are underinvesting to boost short-term profits. The normal statistic trotted out is that buy backs and dividends are now higher than earnings or Research \u0026amp; Development in the US. But when you look more closely you see that R\u0026amp;D has stayed fairly constant as a percentage of GDP and percentage of sales. Rather companies are returning cash as the windfall gains of incredibly cheap borrowing costs due to quantitative easing is being handed back to shareholders. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003ePut another way, QE has not been as effective as the central bankers’ fair weather models suggested. Companies are simply not seeing a wide range of \u003ccontent id=\"48335160-5265-11e7-a1f2-db19572361bb\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003enew investment opportunities \u003c/content\u003ejust because of cheap money. Moreover, QE also means investors are yield hungry and so nudge companies to up their payouts.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eA more compelling concern is the rising share of passive investing risks creating a swath of absentee landlords with less check on poor management teams. Given passive investing is likely to breach half of US equities fund investments in the next 12 months and well over one quarter in Europe, some are becoming more apprehensive.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eWhile there are many tensions and stakeholders running a public company, some management teams are too focused on gilding their cage. Others underemphasise the long-term sustainability of their firms beyond their tenure, get stuck on the treadmill of quarterly reporting or simply chase poorly designed incentive schemes. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eSo who is going to solve this? Rather than passing new laws, we should welcome activist and engaged investors which can be an important catalyst for change. The best academic study on activism suggests activists are not myopic. Harvard’s Lucian Bebchuk and colleagues\u003ca href=\"https://corpgov.law.harvard.edu/2017/01/26/dealing-with-activist-hedge-funds-and-other-activist-investors/\"\u003e looked at 2000 interventions by activists \u003c/a\u003ewhich showed that five years after activist intervention, their operating performance was materially improved. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThat is not to say there aren’t short-term activists, or that all engaged investors proposals are good. There are virtues and vices in short and long-termism. Rather the devil is in the detail and idealism needs to be married with deep pragmatism. A keen focus on appropriate incentives and corporate governance is a critical ingredient. But so too is a pluralistic market for financing the economy: private and public markets; passive and activist investors. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eI often suspect companies get the shareholders they deserve. Those who are not sufficiently focused on driving long-term value and the sustainability of their business ironically make themselves more vulnerable to the activists they It had “given the banks time to present capital plans, but the banks had been unable to offer credible solutions going forward”. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eItalian authorities have wanted to protect senior creditors and depositors to avoid fallout for the Veneto region, where tens of thousands of local people invested in the lenders.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe European Commission said Italian authorities must now “determine the way forward for the two banks in line with Italian insolvency law”. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBrussels said it was in “constructive discussions” with Rome on the next steps and that “good progress is being made to find a solution very soon”, adding that the banks’ depositors “remain fully protected in line with EU rules”.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003ePaolo Gentiloni, Italy’s prime minister, called a cabinet meeting to approve a decree for liquidation of the banks. The government said it would meet at the weekend to ensure measures to protect deposit holders and senior bondholders.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ccontent data-embedded=\"true\" id=\"b4d556f9-bca4-4df5-8ce1-a812118c63e5\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Video\"\u003e\u003c/content\u003e\n\u003cbr/\u003e\n\n\u003cp\u003eThe announcement follows wrangling between Brussels and Rome over how to deal with the struggling lenders without falling foul of EU limits on state aid. Those limits require some capital to come from private sources before a government bailout can be undertaken. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eItaly’s finance ministry and other lenders in the country had held talks in recent weeks over how to cover the banks’ incurred losses of €1.2bn. The talks were driven by fears that failure would have a knock-on effect on the stability of the Italian financial system, which is plagued by \u003ccontent id=\"03048068-5031-11e7-a1f2-db19572361bb\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003elarge amounts of non-performing loans\u003c/content\u003e. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003ccontent id=\"3f0529cc-55d2-11e7-80b6-9bfa4c1f83d2\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eIntesa Sanpaolo\u003c/content\u003e — Italy’s largest domestic bank and considered among its healthiest — said it would approve a \u003ccontent id=\"503fdb83-f34b-3eb5-a545-fa521a991b86\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003edeal to buy the “good” assets\u003c/content\u003e of its troubled smaller rivals for a token price of €1, on the condition the deal had no impact on its core capital ratio or dividend policy. The purchase would exclude bad loans, the cost of at least 4,000 lay-offs and legal risks.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBankers estimate the wind-down could cost about €10bn for Italian taxpayers and the Italian financial system, which may have to contribute to a deposit insurance fund.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003crelated id=\"e869e23c-528f-11e7-bfb8-997009366969\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003e\u003ctitle\u003eLex\u003c/title\u003e\u003cheadline\u003eItalian banks: unto the breach\n\u003c/headline\u003e\u003cmedia\u003e\u003ccontent data-embedded=\"true\" id=\"f91d7eba-53f5-11e7-1ed0-0c60966b289b\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/ImageSet\"\u003e\u003c/content\u003e\u003c/media\u003e\u003cintro\u003e\u003cp\u003eTakeovers are do-able but unlikely\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/intro\u003e\u003c/related\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe future of the Veneto banks has hung in the balance over the past two years since ECB regulators uncovered a capital hole caused by a surge in bad loans, which compounded a financial mis-selling scandal.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe length of time it has taken for regulators to come to any decision on the banks has also raised serious questions about their supervision. Deposit flight at the banks has accelerated as regulators debated how to proceed, according to people informed on the matter.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe impending action to deal with the Veneto banks follows the winding down of four small banks in central Italy and the sale of their good assets to Italian midsized lender \u003cconcept id=\"36b9e13b-2005-3775-a415-02f185c6486a\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/company/PublicCompany\"\u003eUBI Banca\u003c/concept\u003e. {"uuid":"99d7d6c2-8f21-11e3-be85-00144feab7de","content":{"accessLevel":"free","alternativeStandfirsts":{"promotionalStandfirst":"Many developing nations have higher ratios of female executives than advanced countries, but change is slow at the bottom"},"alternativeTitles":{"contentPackageTitle":null,"promotionalTitle":"Growth helps narrow gender gap"},"body":"\u003cbody\u003e\u003ccontent data-embedded=\"true\" id=\"9ae2e8fc-a404-11e3-16d6-978e959e1c97\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/ImageSet\"\u003e\u003c/content\u003e\u003cp\u003eWomen cheered, some men booed, but everyone took notice when \u003ca href=\"http://www.ft.com/intl/topics/people/Christine_Lagarde\" title=\"Christine Lagarde related stories - FT.com\"\u003eChristine Lagarde\u003c/a\u003e quipped in 2010 that had Lehman Brothers been Lehman Sisters, the global financial crisis might have looked different.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIt was only half a joke. As Ms Lagarde, France’s finance minister at the time and now head of the International Monetary Fund, later wrote: “When women are called to action in times of turbulence, it is often on account of their composure, sense of responsibility and great pragmatism in delicate situations.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003ePerhaps surprisingly, it is an argument that emerging markets have recognised better than the developed world. Amid a growing international debate on bridging the \u003ccontent id=\"a122c63c-8e61-11e3-98c6-00144feab7de\" title=\"Women’s salaries dragged down by domestic demands - FT.com\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003egender gap\u003c/content\u003e, the rapid economic growth of emerging markets in recent years has greatly increased \u003ccontent id=\"41dbff1a-8cfc-11e3-ad57-00144feab7de\" title=\"Mobiles aid Africa’s women farmers - FT.com\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eopportunities for women\u003c/content\u003e in business, giving them a boost over their counterparts in developed countries.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThis would seem to contradict perceptions dominant in the west of women in developing nations. The more prevalent image is that tradition and social mores in many of these countries, as well as educational and economic disadvantages, limit women’s empowerment in business.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eTo some extent the perceptions reflect reality: although women lead Argentina, Brazil and Chile, 70 per cent of executives in \u003ccontent id=\"6519773e-8d90-11e3-9dbb-00144feab7de\" title=\"Machismo persists in Latin America despite rise of female presidents - FT.com\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eLatin America\u003c/content\u003e say family pressures cause women to leave their jobs, according to \u003ca href=\"http://www.mckinsey.com/insights/organization/why_top_management_eludes_women_in_latin_america_mckinsey_global_survey_results\" title=\"Why top management eludes women in Latin America: McKinsey Global Survey results - McKinsey.com\"\u003ea survey by McKinsey\u003c/a\u003e, the consultancy.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn \u003ccontent id=\"3549d674-97cd-11e3-ab60-00144feab7de\" title=\"Nora Wu: ‘I admit I considered giving up – but I never did’ - FT.com\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eChina\u003c/content\u003e, women are still expected to put their families first, an attitude reinforced by the \u003ccontent id=\"e10a2290-6fb2-11e3-8352-00144feabdc0\" title=\"China formally eases one-child policy - FT.com\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eone-child policy\u003c/content\u003e. Chinese women are also expected to take care of the elderly in the family.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e“Observers in the west tend to see third-world women as victims,” write Sylvia Ann Hewlett and Ripa Rashid in their 2011 book \u003cem\u003eWinning The War for Talent in Emerging Markets: Why Women are the Solution\u003c/em\u003e. “In a similar vein, business leaders tend not to have women on their radar screen. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e“The fact is that no company can afford to ignore highly qualified female talent if it wants to compete in these fast-expanding economies – and win,” the authors say.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpromo-box\u003e\u003cpromo-title\u003e\u003cp\u003eShare your top tip\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/promo-title\u003e\u003cpromo-image\u003e\u003ccontent data-embedded=\"true\" id=\"b20c435c-7a15-11e3-1c77-978e959e1689\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/ImageSet\"\u003e\u003c/content\u003e\u003c/promo-image\u003e\u003cpromo-intro\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhat is the best advice you received as a woman in business? Share your thoughts in the comment box below or tweet using #FTwomen\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/promo-intro\u003e\u003c/promo-box\u003e\u003cp\u003eAccording to Grant Thornton’s 2014 Women in Business report, which surveyed about 6,600 privately owned companies in 45 countries, the proportion of senior roles filled by women across the Brics countries (Brazil, \u003ccontent id=\"a98d23b2-9244-11e3-8018-00144feab7de\" title=\"Russia’s women technology chiefs find springboard to big time - FT.com\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eRussia\u003c/content\u003e, \u003ccontent id=\"1031b5cc-8e55-11e3-98c6-00144feab7de\" title=\"Senior women executives in India find strength in numbers - FT.com\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eIndia\u003c/content\u003e, China and \u003ccontent id=\"8c60db16-9d76-11e3-83c5-00144feab7de\" title=\"South Africa mining sector offers women fast-track careers - FT.com\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eSouth Africa\u003c/content\u003e) exceeds 30 per cent, compared with about 20 per cent in the G7 group of industrialised nations, and higher than the 24 per cent global average. Some statistics are striking: in China more than 60 per cent of chief financial officers are women.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAcross the Brics, the percentage of companies that have no women in senior roles has fallen from 39 per cent last year to 18 per cent this year. In the boardroom too, women in emerging markets score better than the global average.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThis special report sheds further light on that progress, with data showing that in China about 30 per cent of entrepreneurs are women. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eSix out of \u003ca href=\"http://www.forbes.com/pictures/emeg45gedi/sara-blakely-2/\" title=\"The Self-Made Women Billionaires Of 2013 - Forbes.com\"\u003e24 self-made female billionaires worldwide on the Forbes 2013 list\u003c/a\u003e are from China (including Hong Kong), more than any other country outside the US.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn \u003ccontent id=\"71b2da26-8e58-11e3-98c6-00144feab7de\" title=\"Women’s opportunities in Turkey hinge on school and marriage - FT.com\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eTurkey\u003c/content\u003e, meanwhile, 12 per cent of chief executives are women, according to the World Bank, higher than the EU average.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e“Emerging markets do seem really to value some of the things that women bring to boards and senior roles,” says Francesca Lagerberg, global head of tax at Grant Thornton. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpull-quote\u003e\u003cpull-quote-text\u003e\u003cp\u003eWith large, fast-growing companies you go from [recruiting for] 50 to 500 posts. The need for talent overcomes barriers\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/pull-quote-text\u003e\u003cpull-quote-source\u003eSaadia Zahidi, World Economic Forum\u003c/pull-quote-source\u003e\u003c/pull-quote\u003e\u003cp\u003e“The approach to business is different and there’s a real recognition that innovation and creativity are sometimes more closely linked to female leaders.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eWhat lies behind the numbers? One factor is education: as the authors of \u003cem\u003eWinning the War For Talent\u003c/em\u003e point out, women in emerging markets are graduating from universities and graduate schools at rates that match and often outstrip those of men.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFamily and tradition can work to women’s advantage. In China, and in countries of the former Soviet Union, women’s participation in the labour force has been encouraged; in conservative societies close-knit extended families and affordable help can make it easier for women to work.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe need for women to contribute to the household income has been a driver of ambition – but more jobs also have been available. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eSaadia Zahidi, head of gender parity and human capital at the World Economic Forum, says: “If you have large, fast-growing companies – say you go from 50 to 500 posts [in a company] – the need for talent overcomes barriers that may exist.” \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eInstitutional backing for working women has also helped, with growing support for remote working arrangements, flexible hours and paid maternity leave. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eImposing quotas can be less controversial than in Europe. Ms Lagerberg says more and more business leaders in emerging markets say they back board quotas for women. “It’s telling that people engaged in this are beginning to see where it should go. It might be that quotas come in several years’ time, but business leaders are becoming more conscious about process,” she says.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThere is still a long way to go. And the risk is that the progress achieved so far will not be accelerated as \u003ca href=\"http://www.ft.com/intl/indepth/emerging-markets-in-retreat\" title=\"Emerging markets in retreat in depth - FT.com\"\u003egrowth in emerging economies begins to slow\u003c/a\u003e. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFar too many women still face social pressures to leave their jobs after having children; their entrepreneurial drive is also stifled by constrained access to credit. The wage gap should also narrow. Even if they might receive equal pay to men when they join the labour force, women find the disparity widens as they move up the organisation.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMs Lagerberg cites the need for more role models and mentoring to push women up the corporate ladder. “The more you see senior women in role modelling positions, the more you inspire others to come through. You need [mentoring] programmes with a specific diversity element.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eOther experts warn that improvements at the top do not necessarily mean pro­gress at the bottom. The picture for senior women in emerging markets may be brighter than in the west, but women’s participation in the labour force is lower than in Europe or the US.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMs Zahidi says that in rapidly growing economies such as Brazil, China and Indonesia, women are joining the labour force at relatively faster rates than men.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBut in India, women’s participation in the workforce has risen only 4 per cent, to 34 per cent, in the past eight years. “In India, a policy was just passed [which means] all publicly listed companies have to have one female director on board …and there’s a trend to get more women in leadership positions.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e“But this is also the country where a lot of the poverty and illiteracy is concentrated among females,” Ms Zahidi says. “Because of cultural implications you see a divergence between low- and high-income women.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e——————————————-\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ccontent data-embedded=\"true\" id=\"904d4072-a404-11e3-16d6-978e959e1c97\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/ImageSet\"\u003e\u003c/content\u003e\u003cp\u003e\n\u003cstrong\u003eChanda Kochhar, chief executive, ICICI Bank\u003c/strong\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e‘Change will be driven as much from an economic as from a social perspective. {"uuid":"10c63024-5866-11e7-9fed-c19e2700005f","content":{"accessLevel":"subscribed","alternativeStandfirsts":{"promotionalStandfirst":"Veneto Banca and Banca Popolare di Vicenza to be wound down"},"alternativeTitles":{"contentPackageTitle":null,"promotionalTitle":"ECB confirms struggling Italian banks to close"},"body":"\u003cbody\u003e\u003ccontent data-embedded=\"true\" id=\"a8f8f32c-5866-11e7-1ed0-0c60966b289b\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/ImageSet\"\u003e\u003c/content\u003e\u003cp\u003eTwo of Italy’s banks are set to be wound down after months of clashes between Brussels and Rome over their fate, leaving the eurozone’s third-largest economy facing another test of the stability of its financial system. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cconcept id=\"3a363e59-6e06-37de-8516-e702e6a50382\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/company/PublicCompany\"\u003eVeneto Banca\u003c/concept\u003e and \u003cconcept id=\"15a7b142-613e-3202-9b24-a4d06ec0fa18\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/company/PublicCompany\"\u003eBanca Popolare di Vicenza\u003c/concept\u003e, two mid-sized lenders based in the country’s prosperous industrial north-east, will be wound down by the Italian authorities after the European Central Bank confirmed on Friday evening that the two lenders were “failing or likely to fail”. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eItaly has succeeded in protecting senior creditors in the banks having gained EU backing for a wind down of the lenders under Italian law rather than new European banking rules.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe ECB, which supervises Europe’s largest lenders, said the two banks had “repeatedly breached supervisory capital requirements”. It had “given the banks time to present capital plans, but the banks had been unable to offer credible solutions going forward”. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eItalian authorities have wanted to protect senior creditors and depositors to avoid fallout for the Veneto region, where tens of thousands of local people invested in the lenders.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe European Commission said Italian authorities must now “determine the way forward for the two banks in line with Italian insolvency law”. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBrussels said it was in “constructive discussions” with Rome on the next steps and that “good progress is being made to find a solution very soon”, adding that the banks’ depositors “remain fully protected in line with EU rules”.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003ePaolo Gentiloni, Italy’s prime minister, called a cabinet meeting to approve a decree for liquidation of the banks. The government said it would meet at the weekend to ensure measures to protect deposit holders and senior bondholders.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ccontent data-embedded=\"true\" id=\"b4d556f9-bca4-4df5-8ce1-a812118c63e5\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Video\"\u003e\u003c/content\u003e\n\u003cbr/\u003e\n\n\u003cp\u003eThe announcement follows wrangling between Brussels and Rome over how to deal with the struggling lenders without falling foul of EU limits on state aid. Those limits require some capital to come from private sources before a government bailout can be undertaken. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eItaly’s finance ministry and other lenders in the country had held talks in recent weeks over how to cover the banks’ incurred losses of €1.2bn. The talks were driven by fears that failure would have a knock-on effect on the stability of the Italian financial system, which is plagued by \u003ccontent id=\"03048068-5031-11e7-a1f2-db19572361bb\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003elarge amounts of non-performing loans\u003c/content\u003e. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003ccontent id=\"3f0529cc-55d2-11e7-80b6-9bfa4c1f83d2\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eIntesa Sanpaolo\u003c/content\u003e — Italy’s largest domestic bank and considered among its healthiest — said it would approve a \u003ccontent id=\"503fdb83-f34b-3eb5-a545-fa521a991b86\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003edeal to buy the “good” assets\u003c/content\u003e of its troubled smaller rivals for a token price of €1, on the condition the deal had no impact on its core capital ratio or dividend policy. The purchase would exclude bad loans, the cost of at least 4,000 lay-offs and legal risks.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBankers estimate the wind-down could cost about €10bn for Italian taxpayers and the Italian financial system, which may have to contribute to a deposit insurance fund.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003crelated id=\"e869e23c-528f-11e7-bfb8-997009366969\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003e\u003ctitle\u003eLex\u003c/title\u003e\u003cheadline\u003eItalian banks: unto the breach\n\u003c/headline\u003e\u003cmedia\u003e\u003ccontent data-embedded=\"true\" id=\"f91d7eba-53f5-11e7-1ed0-0c60966b289b\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/ImageSet\"\u003e\u003c/content\u003e\u003c/media\u003e\u003cintro\u003e\u003cp\u003eTakeovers are do-able but unlikely\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/intro\u003e\u003c/related\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe future of the Veneto banks has hung in the balance over the past two years since ECB regulators uncovered a capital hole caused by a surge in bad loans, which compounded a financial mis-selling scandal.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe length of time it has taken for regulators to come to any decision on the banks has also raised serious questions about their supervision. Deposit flight at the banks has accelerated as regulators debated how to proceed, according to people informed on the matter.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe impending action to deal with the Veneto banks follows the winding down of four small banks in central Italy and the sale of their good assets to Italian midsized lender \u003cconcept id=\"36b9e13b-2005-3775-a415-02f185c6486a\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/company/PublicCompany\"\u003eUBI Banca\u003c/concept\u003e. {"uuid":"8a8e24dc-b86d-11e1-a2d6-00144feabdc0","content":{"accessLevel":"subscribed","alternativeStandfirsts":{"promotionalStandfirst":"Hong Kong exchange sees LME as significant driver of future profit"},"alternativeTitles":{"contentPackageTitle":null,"promotionalTitle":null},"body":"\u003cbody\u003e\u003cp\u003eWinning a marathon is usually a cause for celebration. But after a nine-month\u003ccontent id=\"6fdca33c-b54a-11e1-ad93-00144feabdc0\" title=\"HKEx agrees to buy LME for £1.4bn - FT.com\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003e race to acquire the London Metal Exchange\u003c/content\u003e, shareholders of Hong Kong Exchanges \u0026amp; Clearing may be less than jubilant.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAt a price of £1.39bn, it would take 180 years for HKEx to recoup its investment if the 135-year-old exchange carries on earning at last year’s rate.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e“Clearly, from a multiple on current earnings this is a tough one to defend,” says Alex Kramm, exchanges analyst at UBS. A person close to the bid concedes: “On conventional metrics it’s pretty scary.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eNonetheless, the LME has had no shortage of suitors willing to pay up. Behind HKEx, Atlanta-based IntercontinentalExchange was bidding just below £1.3bn, while CME Group, \u003ccontent id=\"0cd04c1e-a404-11e1-8878-00144feabdc0\" title=\"CME knocked out of race for LME - FT.com\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eknocked out last month\u003c/content\u003e, was also willing to pay more than £1bn, according to people familiar with the situation.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e“Everybody who is anybody in the commodities exchanges [industry] has been around this price,” Charles Li, chief executive of HKEx, told the Financial Times.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe desire to buy the LME is in part strategic: as one of the last member-owned exchanges in the world, and the undisputed centre for global metals trading, the LME has long been coveted by the industry’s heavyweights. “It is a strategic asset. A lot of people wanted this business,” says a person close to the HKEx bid.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBut HKEx must still show it can make money from the LME, which is currently run, as one veteran puts it, “like a golf club – for the benefit of the members”.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eUnderlying the valuation lies a conviction that HKEx can transform the LME from a clubby institution into a significant driver of its profits.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003ePart of the process has begun, with a plan to raise fees, which will lift last year’s profits from £7.7m to £23.8m according to HKEx, and to bring clearing in-house, which could add about £20m to earnings, analysts say.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBut that is not enough alone to justify the price HKEx will pay – if LME shareholders and UK regulators approve the deal. The key to the Hong Kong group’s bid for the LME is a big bet on China.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe country is the largest consumer of metals traded on the LME, but few Chinese companies can access the exchange. The LME only recently approved the country’s first member, Bank of China, and HKEx estimates that only 20-25 per cent of trades on the LME originate from China.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e“Substantial trading and hedging needs from Asia are not currently met through the system,” says Mr Li.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eTo address that, HKEx would launch a suite of new services tailored for the Chinese market, in particular allowing Chinese traders to clear their trades during the Asian day, instead of having to wait until early afternoon in London.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe \u003ca href=\"http://www.ft.com/intl/global-renminbi\" title=\"Special report - Renminbi: Global Challenge - FT.com\"\u003einternationalisation of the renminbi\u003c/a\u003e would also be a boost. This allows HKEx to launch clearing, and later trading, of metals in the Chinese currency, with huge flows through Hong Kong and HKEx using the LME as “another net to catch fish”, as one person close to the bid said.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eRomnesh Lamba, HKEx’s head of market development and a key architect of the deal, said: “To us clearing is not just about a revenue and income stream. It’s not a huge component of the valuation but it’s really important to facilitating China and new products.” \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eHKEx plans to launch new products – of which coking coal, used for steelmaking, and stainless steel are likely to be priorities.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFor all that, some remain sceptical. Investors are losing their enthusiasm for the “\u003ccontent id=\"099cf10e-a964-11e1-9772-00144feabdc0\" title=\"A less-super commodities supercycle - FT.com\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003ecommodities supercycle\u003c/content\u003e”, which could lead to a fall in trading volumes. So could higher interest rates, which reduce traders’ ability to make large trades.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eKenneth Worthington, analyst at JPMorgan, values the LME at $1.9bn on the most bullish assumptions. “As they pay a higher and higher price, the margin for error continues to go down and the risk is increasingly borne by the bidder’s rather than LME’s shareholders,” he says.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIndeed, HKEx’s shares have fallen about 25 per cent since February, in part over concern that it would overpay for the LME. On Friday, shares in ICE, the other finalist in the LME bidding war, jumped nearly 5 per cent, amid relief that it had not been successful.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBut HKEx executives were triumphant on Friday night at a cocktail party to celebrate the 12th anniversary of the exchange’s listing on its own market. They were unconcerned about any reduction in HKEx’s earnings over the next two years.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e“This is not going to happen overnight,” said Mr Li. “But we are highly confident”.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eAdditional reporting by Robert Cookson in Hong Kong and Jeremy Grant in {"uuid":"b60cd0fe-caf3-39f8-a620-812ccbce55ba","content":{"accessLevel":"registered","body":"\u003cbody\u003e\u003cp\u003eFirst it was Indonesia, then South Korea. Inspired by the strength of  an Asian recovery that has left the western developed world standing,  regional central bankers are challenging old Western orthodoxies, and are \u003ca href=\"http://www.ft.com/cms/s/0/feb03e14-828b-11df-85ba-00144feabdc0.html\"\u003eembracing once dreaded capital controls\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIs Thailand about to become the latest country to join them?  No. But that hasn’t stopped the new central bank governor from talking about it.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eTarisa Watanagase, the governor of Bank of Thailand, said yesterday that while the bank had no imminent plans to introduce capital controls, many were questioning the conventional thinking on the subject matter.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cblockquote\u003e\u003cp\u003eCapital controls were previously dismissed as something old fashioned, something that interferes with the market mechanism and should not be an acceptable tool of a central bank and I think that idea has changed.\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/blockquote\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIt is no surprise this newest trend is gaining force in Asia, as global liquidity from developed countries \u003ca href=\"http://blogs.ft.com/beyond-brics/2010/07/20/fund-inflows-boost-emerging-markets/\"\u003esloshes into the region\u003c/a\u003e and central bankers, increasingly concerned about controlling rising inflation and looming asset bubbles, look for a policy toolkit to control these inflows. Thailand’s central bank governor is just the latest to publicly challenge the status quo.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFor Tarisa, capital flows from western economies where interest rates are at record lows, “could attract de-stabilizing capital inflows that will put pressure on exchange rate, encourage a buildup of financial institutions’ short-term debt, and fuel bubbles in various sectors.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAnd it’s not just Asian central bankers that see controls as a necessary evil. The International Monetary Fund is also cautiously supportive of the growing trend in Asia.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eManaging director of the IMF, Dominique Strauss-Kahn, \u003ca href=\"http://www.ft.com/cms/s/0/5ba241aa-8ce9-11df-bad7-00144feab49a.html\"\u003elast week told the FT\u003c/a\u003e that he was “sympathetic” to emerging countries embracing controls as a last resort to counter foreign investors inflating asset bubbles, but warned,:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cblockquote\u003e\u003cp\u003eYou have to be very pragmatic. Long-term capital controls are certainly not a good thing… But short-term capital controls may be necessary in some cases: it is a matter of balancing the costs of different options.\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/blockquote\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe latest data from the Asian Development Bank is likely to give governments further justification for consdering capital controls. ADB data released yesterday suggests 2010 growth in developing Asia is stronger it previously forecast in April. Regional GDP is now forecast 4 percentage points higher, at 7.9 per cent for 2010. This, according to the ADB, is due to better-than-expected results in the first quarter—driven by buoyant exports, strong private demand, and sustained stimulus policy effects.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBut what Asian governments are most likely to focus on is the ADB’s caution of the downside risks to growth:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cblockquote\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe economic outlook is subject to three major risks: (i) a disruption in the recovery in advanced economies; (ii) destabilizing capital flows; and (iii) unintended policy errors or an inappropriate policy mix when unwinding stimulus.\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/blockquote\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIt’s exactly this ‘destabilizing capital flows’ that will give Asian central bankers justification for tighter controls.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAsian governments, as the victims of the 1997 financial crisis, had an  inherent suspicion of the Greenspan-like faith in markets long before the  latest crash, and the new orthodoxy on capital controls will suit them  just fine.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eRelated reading:\u003c/strong\u003e\u003cbr/\u003e\n\u003ca ADB data released yesterday suggests 2010 growth in developing Asia is stronger it previously forecast in April. Regional GDP is now forecast 4 percentage points higher, at 7.9 per cent for 2010. This, according to the ADB, is due to better-than-expected results in the first quarter—driven by buoyant exports, strong private demand, and sustained stimulus policy effects.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBut what Asian governments are most likely to focus on is the ADB’s caution of the downside risks to growth:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cblockquote\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe economic outlook is subject to three major risks: (i) a disruption in the recovery in advanced economies; (ii) destabilizing capital flows; and (iii) unintended policy errors or an inappropriate policy mix when unwinding stimulus.\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/blockquote\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIt’s exactly this ‘destabilizing capital flows’ that will give Asian central bankers justification for tighter controls.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAsian governments, as the victims of the 1997 financial crisis, had an  inherent suspicion of the Greenspan-like faith in markets long before the  latest crash, and the new orthodoxy on capital controls will suit them  just fine.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eRelated reading:\u003c/strong\u003e\u003cbr/\u003e\n\u003ca href=\"http://blogs.ft.com/beyond-brics/2010/07/06/malaysian-rates-to-raise-or-not-to-raise-that-is-the-question/\"\u003eMalaysian rates: to raise or not to raise, that is the question, FT href=\"http://www.ft.com/cms/s/0/feb03e14-828b-11df-85ba-00144feabdc0.html\"\u003eembracing once dreaded capital controls\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIs Thailand about to become the yesterday that while the bank had no imminent plans to introduce capital controls, many were questioning the conventional thinking on the subject {"uuid":"4b380a33-9aa8-4267-9062-90aa1ffa6ac0","content":{"accessLevel":"free","alternativeStandfirsts":{"promotionalStandfirst":"Tech companies race to develop the dominant ‘virtual assistant’"},"alternativeTitles":{"contentPackageTitle":null,"promotionalTitle":"Artificial intelligence: Designs for life"},"body":"\u003cbody\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003ca data-asset-type=\"slideshow\" data-embedded=\"true\" href=\"http://www.ft.com/cms/s/50635b88-b941-11e4-98f6-00144feab7de.html#slide0\"\u003e\u003c/a\u003eHow smart do you want your smartphone to be? In designing \u003ccontent id=\"9eaf977c-341a-11e4-b81c-00144feabdc0\" title=\"Microsoft brings mobile personal assistant Cortana to UK and China - FT.com\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eCortana, the voice-activated “virtual assistant”\u003c/content\u003e built into its mobile software, Microsoft is betting that most people are not yet ready to hand too much control of their lives to an artificial brain.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eA \u003ccontent id=\"5ae55b5e-b047-11e4-a2cc-00144feab7de\" title=\"The strange world of the humans who loaned their voices to Siri - FT.com\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003esoft-voiced presence \u003c/content\u003ewith a slightly sassy attitude drawn from a video game character, Cortana is quite capable of reading your email to see if you have a flight coming up, then using the information to tell you when it is time to leave for the airport.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBut Microsoft will not let “her” take the liberty. Instead, the system asks permission, like a discreet human assistant who does not want to assume too much — a step that also helps to confirm the software is on the right track in anticipating your wishes.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e“At the moment it’s progressive intelligence, not autonomous intelligence,” says Marcus Ash, group program manager for Cortana, which is enabled on phones with the Windows operating system, including Microsoft’s Lumia devices. People do not want to be surprised by how much their phones are starting to take over, he says: “We made an explicit decision to be a little less ‘magical’ and a little more transparent.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eNiceties like this could soon be a thing of the past. The race is on between some of the biggest tech companies to come up with omniscient guides capable of filtering the \u003ccontent id=\"912327be-7564-11e4-a1a9-00144feabdc0\" title=\"Smartphones will become our electronic assistants - FT.com\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003ecomplex digital world\u003c/content\u003e\n. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eLike the browser wars of the 1990s, the outcome will help to set the balance of power in the next phase of the internet. By channelling attention and making decisions on behalf of their users, virtual assistants will have enormous power to make or break many other businesses. Many companies — from carmakers to entertainment concerns — aim to develop voice-powered assistants of their own to keep their customers loyal. But the future may belong instead to a handful of all-knowing assistants, much as Google’s search engine managed to suck in so many of the world’s queries on the web.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThough it has not reached the point of mass adoption yet, the potential of this new form of artificial intelligence has all the tech companies scrambling. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAlong with \u003cconcept id=\"0b09e7fb-d889-3879-a1c2-2dd0272943e3\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/company/PublicCompany\"\u003eMicrosoft\u003c/concept\u003e, they include \u003cconcept id=\"2384fa7a-d514-3d6a-a0ea-3a711f66d0d8\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/company/PublicCompany\"\u003eApple\u003c/concept\u003e, with the Siri question-and-answer service on its iPhones and iPads, \u003cconcept id=\"410dd646-0e14-3e4a-83ea-732bed4a1453\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/company/PublicCompany\"\u003eGoogle\u003c/concept\u003e, whose Google Now service tries to anticipate its users’ information needs, as well as \u003cconcept id=\"36d2852b-1cb9-3c71-936e-8d755747048f\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/company/PublicCompany\"\u003eFacebook\u003c/concept\u003e, \u003cconcept id=\"23629959-5137-384a-a8af-3f7d9cb16912\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/company/PublicCompany\"\u003eAmazon\u003c/concept\u003e and \u003cconcept id=\"9e714df6-b0de-3dc0-b0b6-a7c32f66cf48\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/company/PublicCompany\"\u003eBaidu\u003c/concept\u003e, which are experimenting with mobile and desktop applications.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe tech world is in the grip of an “AI spring”, says Oren Etzioni, head of the artificial intelligence research institute backed by Microsoft co-founder Paul Allen. Of all the new fronts this has opened up, the development of virtual assistants has become “one of the most exciting,” he says.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe consequences will stretch far beyond the ability to speak a question into your smartphone and get an immediate answer, or to have warnings about heavy traffic “pushed” to you at the time you normally leave for work. If the virtual assistants catch on, they are likely to take over many of the decisions that litter everyday life as they try to anticipate their users’ needs. And they are likely to have minds of their own.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eReconfiguring services\u003c/strong\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e“It’s really a meta-layer of machine intelligence that’s sitting on top of all the services,” says Mitch Lasky, a partner at Silicon Valley venture capital firm Benchmark and an investor in mobile software company Cyanogen. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e“If I ask Siri to find me a taxi, it may not go to Uber — and I may not be able to force it to.” For the many companies that have come to rely on customers accessing their services over smartphones, the consequences could be significant.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpull-quote\u003e\u003cpull-quote-text\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe industry is still suffering from the hangover from the Siri let-down\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/pull-quote-text\u003e\u003cpull-quote-source\u003eTim Tuttle, chief executive, Expect Labs\u003c/pull-quote-source\u003e\u003c/pull-quote\u003e\u003cp\u003eGiven the limitations that virtual assistants have shown up to now, it might seem premature to start worrying about a time when they suddenly take it on themselves to pre-empt their users’ needs and desires.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eApple’s launch of Siri in 2011 was a catchy demonstration of advances in speech recognition software and question-and-answer systems. But it also served as a demonstration of the limits of the technology, as users quickly found that the system could only handle certain types of query.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e“The industry is still suffering from the hangover from the Siri let-down,” says Tim Tuttle, chief executive of Expect Labs, a company that makes a voice-activated assistant that can be used to power other companies’ applications. He and other experts in the field say that the technology has come a long way in a short space of time.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eVirtual assistants rely on a number of different technologies. One involves speech recognition. Google says it has reduced the error rate for recognising words in its own mobile app to less than 8 per cent — a level at which it says the service has become a practical alternative to entering text.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eApple has also made considerable headway in overcoming the early disappointment by adding more capabilities to Siri and helping users better understand what it can and cannot do.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eA second front has involved the predictive technology for anticipating what a user will want to know next. This draws on contextual data — aspects such as the location and time of day — as well as personal information. Knowing what other people have found useful is also valuable, says Aparna Chennapragada, a director of product management for Google’s mobile app: “For most people in your situation, what are the top five things you need?”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe tech companies do not release much information about the extent to which the virtual assistants are catching on. But what there is suggests that users are starting to show interest — even though, as Mr Etzioni cautions, it is probably too early to call it “an inflection point” for the technology. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eChinese search company Baidu, for instance, puts the proportion of its searches that are conducted by voice at nearly 10 per cent. And Google says the number of times its users spoke a search query into their smartphones doubled last year, though it does not disclose a figure. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe pick-up in use has some predicting that it will not be long before talking to gadgets becomes second nature — particularly as computer intelligence spreads to many new types of device where entering text is impractical, from \u003ccontent id=\"bbea6a02-ab92-11e4-8070-00144feab7de\" title=\"Google hopes all-or-nothing bet on robot cars will pay off soon - FT.com\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003esmartwatches to televisions and cars\u003c/content\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eVoice interactions like these “will be the gateway for all applications and the way into everything,” says Mr Tuttle. If virtual assistants live up to the industry’s hopes, then whoever has control of this new gateway will end up with considerable power.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eBespoke or all-round assistance?\u003c/strong\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eA key question determining the outcome will be whether the general-purpose digital assistants of the tech giants become dominant in steering people through this new world, or whether users will gravitate to more narrowly focused intelligent guides to help them navigate specialist applications.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ccontent data-embedded=\"true\" id=\"c7eae938-bac1-11e4-1a21-978e959e1c97\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/ImageSet\"\u003e\u003c/content\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe big tech companies “want one intelligent interface to rule them all”, says Mr Tuttle. But that “is not what all the other app makers and device makers want at all.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eSome companies are already starting to experiment with embedding virtual assistants inside their own mobile apps. In the US, pizza chain \u003cconcept id=\"6dd3c298-3877-372f-86bb-7f651db6dcf3\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/company/PublicCompany\"\u003eDomino’s\u003c/concept\u003e has even run a television advertising campaign to promote “Dom”, a digital assistant that guides customers through the process of ordering a pizza on their smartphones.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eLike others trying out the technology, Domino’s hopes that personality will play a part in drawing in customers: “He’s fun, but very focused on the pizza ordering experience,” Dennis Maloney, head of multimedia marketing, said when Dom was unveiled to the world.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e“Consumers want to interact with brands on their phones,” he predicted, leading general-purpose assistants such as Siri to “redirect” users to specialist guides like Dom when they want to deal with a particular company.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e“The individual app makers can always understand and navigate content in their own apps better,” adds Mr Tuttle. His company’s technology is already being used to help TV viewers search through video-on-demand services and will soon be embedded in cars, whose makers he describes as loath to surrender their customers to Google and Apple.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBut as ways of interacting with smartphones and the many new devices of the “internet of things” change, that kind of brand loyalty may start to erode.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMr Etzioni describes the kind of instruction that the next generation of personal assistants will be asked to handle: “Book me a table for three in an excellent restaurant downtown with easy parking.” \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eTo fulfil that desire, the technology would need not only a high level of natural language recognition, but the ability to draw information from different services (a restaurant review site, a maps app) and issue instructions through a reservation system.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThis kind of “more sophisticated service composition” could start to appear on smartphones in a little more than a year from now, he adds. This points to a post-app world, where pieces of information and service components that currently reside in standalone apps are stitched together to resolve life’s many small, daily problems.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpull-quote\u003e\u003cpull-quote-text\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe acute need to find the right app will become secondary to the need to complete the task\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/pull-quote-text\u003e\u003cpull-quote-source\u003eDag Kittlaus, one of the Siri creators\u003c/pull-quote-source\u003e\u003c/pull-quote\u003e\u003cp\u003eFor users, it would happen invisibly: they might not know, or care, which services had been called on to handle their requests — though the company determining which apps to call on to fulfil their needs would hold considerable sway.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e“The acute need to find the right app will become secondary to the need to complete the task,” says \u003ca href=\"http://blogs.ft.com/tech-blog/2010/02/a-virtual-personal-assistant-for-the-mobile-web/\" title=\"A virtual personal assistant for the mobile web - FT.com\"\u003eDag Kittlaus, one of the creators of Siri\u003c/a\u003e. Mr Kittlaus is now co-founder and chief executive of Viv Labs, a start-up that is trying to build an assistant to handle tasks like this.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e“Intelligent assistants will likewise break free of the notion of an app,” Mr Kittlaus predicts. “Your car can be intelligent. Your refrigerator can be intelligent. Your phone can be intelligent.” As a result, there will be no need to find an app, tap to open it and search inside for what you need: “If you need something, you will simply ask for it.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eLike many ideas in tech, this combining of services on the fly like this is not new. At the height of the last tech boom, Microsoft imagined achieving something similar with a proprietary technology it called Hailstorm. The idea, coming at the height of the company’s power, terrified many in the tech world, who saw it as a way to force others to accept Microsoft’s terms of trade for all digital interactions.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eOutsiders such as Mr Etzioni now credit the software company with having developed a more open view of the world. “It’s an underdog thing — we’re a little bit more humbled than Microsoft was in the past,” says Mr Ash. “Cortana is not disruptive from the point of view of services,” he adds, but is designed to act as an open platform.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003ePulling in data\u003c/strong\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe terms on which app makers would allow their services to interact have yet to be determined, but the first elements of the technology to make this possible are starting to emerge. Last month, Google released an experimental API that lets other companies insert their own content into its Google Now service, so that their information can also be “pushed” at users when it is most likely to be needed.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMost of the big tech companies are also working on methods for “deep linking” content inside standalone apps to make it possible to automatically find — and use — the information in other contexts. This may not turn the world of closed apps into the wide-open world of the hyperlinked web, but it still points to a time when the walls between apps will start to break down.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn this new world, information would flow continuously, reflecting the nature of computing when users are surrounded by always-connected devices, says Google’s Ms Chennapragada: the idea of opening a web page to read something would feel very old-fashioned.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFeeding the process would be new types of real-time and “actionable” information — the kind that helps virtual assistants make quick decisions as they assume more of their users’ everyday tasks. Offering up the right nugget of information or the right offer at the right time will be key to oiling the wheels of this new economy.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFor many publishers and ecommerce companies this would be a very different world, requiring entirely new methods to reach customers. But if the Cortanas and Siris end up ruling the digital roost, they are likely to have little choice.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/body\u003e","brands":[{"id":"http://api.ft.com/things/dbb0bdae-1f0c-11e4-b0cb-b2227cce2b54"}],"byline":"Richard Where I have worries is that he won’t get anything back,” Mr Nuttall, the anti-EU Tory campaigner, told the Financial Times. “Until we have an in-out referendum, the battle will continue. We will go on until the British people have a say; let them decide, not a handful of business people.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMore moderate Tories are fearful that the prime minister’s strategy to engage with the eurosceptic wing will backfire and result in a UK exit should attempts to claw back powers from Brussels fail. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e“He has titillated the party with this wretched Europe speech and having raised those expectations he can’t but disappoint. We really do need a more diverse debate than we have now,” said one pro-European backbencher. You can read \u003ccontent id=\"f449e9b1-1071-3f92-99ae-cf76e3058d33\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eour full news story here\u003c/content\u003e.\u003c/em\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eNow, back to our regularly scheduled programming:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eOne by one, Europe’s biggest consumer food companies are being targeted by aggressive — often activist — investors.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFirst, we had private equity group \u003cstrong\u003e3G \u003c/strong\u003ecoming after \u003cstrong\u003eUnilever \u003c/strong\u003ewith a failed $143bn takeover approach via \u003cstrong\u003eKraft Heinz\u003c/strong\u003e. Unilever went into immediate response mode, boosting its operating profit margin target and initiating the disposal of its spreads business. Now \u003cstrong\u003eNestlé \u003c/strong\u003eis \u003ccontent id=\"723b4a78-59e8-11e7-b553-e2df1b0c3220\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003ein the crosshairs\u003c/content\u003e of \u003cstrong\u003eDan Loeb\u003c/strong\u003e, who is calling on the company to sell its 23 per cent stake in cosmetics maker \u003cstrong\u003eL’Oréal\u003c/strong\u003e, among other things. And DD hears that \u003cstrong\u003eDanone \u003c/strong\u003emay be next.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAs one top consumer banker put it to DD, activists tend to move in herds: “Activists often move as a group in targeting companies. For instance, US consumer companies have been a regular investment area for activists who have invested in \u003cstrong\u003eMondelez\u003c/strong\u003e, \u003cstrong\u003ePepsi\u003c/strong\u003e, \u003cstrong\u003eP\u0026amp;G \u003c/strong\u003eand \u003cstrong\u003eConAgra\u003c/strong\u003e. Activists have also targeted large companies such as \u003cstrong\u003eHoneywell\u003c/strong\u003e, \u003cstrong\u003eApple \u003c/strong\u003eand \u003cstrong\u003eMicrosoft\u003c/strong\u003e. [The] question with Nestlé is whether this will kick off a round of European consumer activist investments.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eHere is \u003ca href=\"http://www.thirdpointpublic.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/06/Third-Point-Nestle-Letter.pdf\"\u003eLoeb’s letter to investors\u003c/a\u003e, in which he calls on Nestlé to set a formal profit margin target of 18-20 per cent by 2020, boost its debt to buy back shares, put non-core products in its portfolio up for sale and offload that L’Oréal stake. Here’s a \u003ccontent id=\"c47b10ae-5a68-11e7-b553-e2df1b0c3220\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003emore detailed analysis\u003c/content\u003e of the move. Lex’s take: \u003ccontent id=\"49be16e2-5a75-11e7-9bc8-8055f264aa8b\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eit’s worth it\u003c/content\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cimg src=\"https://www.ft.com/__origami/service/image/v2/images/raw/https%3A%2F%2Femail-platform-ftcom-manual.s3-eu-west-1.amazonaws.com%2F480abf55-d036-4d4c-b74b-7c0f7425ce6a?source=ft-email-manual\u0026amp;width=1024\u0026amp;fit=scale-down\" width=\"600\"/\u003e\u003cp\u003e\n\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003ePeople close to the situation tell DD that Loeb’s stake is a constructive position — but he hasn’t ruled out asking for changes to the board. They also said that Loeb and one of his partners met with Nestlé chief executive \u003cstrong\u003eMark Schneider\u003c/strong\u003e — who took over in January — before \u003cstrong\u003eThird Point\u003c/strong\u003e disclosed its position and his reaction to their concerns was “polite”. Loeb is said to want Nestlé to be \"decisive and ambitious\" rather than “glacial” and “slow-moving”, these people said.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eHere’s \u003ccontent id=\"723b4a78-59e8-11e7-b553-e2df1b0c3220\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eour news story\u003c/content\u003e on the move, which sent Nestlé shares to a record — and also sent shares in other European consumer groups higher.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eTake a look at the chart below to see Monday’s movement among those groups — L’Oréal, Danone, Unilever and \u003cstrong\u003eDiageo\u003c/strong\u003e — to get a sense for how Loeb’s letter is reverberating across the sector: investors believe that the activist pressure will spread throughout the industry, forcing the companies to increase profitability targets and take other steps to increase shareholder value.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cimg alt=\"\" src=\"https://www.ft.com/__origami/service/image/v2/images/raw/https%3A%2F%2Femail-platform-ftcom-manual.s3-eu-west-1.amazonaws.com%2F488b1309-4db9-4e5d-9c7e-cad7a7636694?source=ft-email-manual\u0026amp;width=1024\u0026amp;fit=scale-down\" width=\"600\"/\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe herd, it seems, is on the move.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3 class=\"ft-subhead\"\u003eCarlyle’s bounty\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIt took about nine months for \u003cstrong\u003eCarlyle\u003c/strong\u003e, the US buyout group, to find a decent offer for \u003cstrong\u003eHolland \u0026amp; Barrett\u003c/strong\u003e, the health foods retailer. At the eleventh hour, \u003cstrong\u003eMikhail Fridman\u003c/strong\u003e, one of the wealthiest businessmen in Russia, \u003ccontent id=\"5727598e-582b-11e7-9fed-c19e2700005f\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eforked over £1.8bn\u003c/content\u003e, elbowing out keen bidders like \u003cstrong\u003eKKR\u003c/strong\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe lengthy auction process stalled amid the complexities of removing H\u0026amp;B, a retailer, from \u003cstrong\u003eNature’s Bounty\u003c/strong\u003e, a wholesaler. “It’s extremely unusual for a wholesaler to own a retailer so these two businesses didn’t belong together,” said a person involved in the sale.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThere were also costs associated in extracting H\u0026amp;B from shared functions with Nature’s Bounty, including IT services.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe proud new owner will now want to cash in on the growing demand in the health food market but to do so, Fridman will have to push harder on digital offerings and update “old and dusty” stores, people close to the deal told DD.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBut the saga continues. Carlyle wanted from the start to sell the two businesses in one lump. Unable to find a buyer it had to go down the route of selling one to \u003cstrong\u003eL1 Retail\u003c/strong\u003e, a fund set up by Fridman last December.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eDD now understands that a potential sale of Nature’s Bounty — which it acquired seven years ago for $3.8bn, including debt — is on the horizon. Even though an IPO had been on the cards, this was not the case for now, a person familiar with the deal said. In fact, the person said, “there are some discussions going on with potential bidders”.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eCarlyle is expecting “someone to come forward with a compelling offer” but if that was not the case, they are thinking of keeping the businesses rather than listing it, the person added. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e“Transactions aren’t timed perfectly but there is an ongoing effort to sell Nature’s Bounty. The situation is very fluid,” the person said. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eWelcome to your curated briefing on deals and dealmaking by the Financial Times. There were some signs that those restrictions were slowly lifting in the second quarter of the year but the latest campaign has showed that China’s top leaders \u003ccontent id=\"19810ea2-5814-11e7-80b6-9bfa4c1f83d2\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003ewill not tolerate aggressive dealmaking\u003c/content\u003e.\u003cbr/\u003e \u003cbr/\u003eThe FT has a handy explainer of why Anbang is at the centre of the crackdown on leverage. \u003ccontent id=\"526ef434-54d3-11e7-9fed-c19e2700005f\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eYou can read that story here\u003c/content\u003e. DD finds the following to be the most compelling reasons for the attention on the company: \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cp\u003eAnbang is big enough and connected enough to pose some systemic risks: its subsidiaries own Rmb1.06tn ($155bn) worth of shares in mainland-listed companies and has completed foreign acquisitions worth more than $11bn since 2014.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cp\u003eIt has funded some of these acquisitions with high-risk high-return insurance products. Regulators have banned Anbang from selling the products, which could prove dangerous: “This product is like heroin. It’s very dangerous, but you can’t give it up all at once . . . Otherwise, you can generate a significant cash crunch,” said Sam Radwan, partner at Enhance.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cp\u003eSome Chinese publications have said Anbang’s equity base is not what it seems: respected Chinese financial news website \u003cstrong\u003eCaixin\u003c/strong\u003e alleged that the group’s $9bn equity injection in 2014 relied on financial sleight of hand and was “fake”. Anbang has denied the charge and said it intended to sue Caixin. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003ch3 class=\"ft-subhead\"\u003eJob moves\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eCitigroup\u003c/strong\u003e has appointed \u003cstrong\u003eToby Ali\u003c/strong\u003e, who was recently at \u003cstrong\u003eBank of America\u003c/strong\u003e, as co-head of the bank’s leveraged finance business in Europe, the Middle East and Africa. Ali joins \u003cstrong\u003eSimon Francis\u003c/strong\u003e, a former \u003cstrong\u003eCredit Suisse\u003c/strong\u003e executive, who was also appointed co-head of leverage finance for the regions earlier this year. Both Francis and Ali will report to the bank’s head of capital markets, \u003cstrong\u003ePhilip Drury\u003c/strong\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eCiti \u003c/strong\u003ealso hired \u003cstrong\u003eKarl-Georg Altenburg\u003c/strong\u003e as a senior adviser for clients in Emea and Germany. He was co-head of corporate finance for Emea at \u003cstrong\u003eDeutsche Bank\u003c/strong\u003e and was also chief executive and chief country officer for \u003cstrong\u003eJPMorgan\u003c/strong\u003e for Germany, Austria and Switzerland. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eStephen Pierce\u003c/strong\u003e, \u003cstrong\u003eGoldman Sachs\u003c/strong\u003e’s global head of equity capital markets, is retiring after 31 years at the group. Pierce advised on some of the biggest deals of the past three decades — including \u003cstrong\u003eGM\u003c/strong\u003e’s $20bn IPO in 2010, \u003cstrong\u003eSquare\u003c/strong\u003e’s 2015 listing and the US Treasury’s selldown of its stake in \u003cstrong\u003eAIG\u003c/strong\u003e. He also helped take his own company public in 1999. And DD hears that \u003cstrong\u003eDanone \u003c/strong\u003emay be next.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAs one top consumer banker put it to DD, activists tend to move in herds: “Activists often move as a group in targeting companies. For instance, US consumer companies have been a regular investment area for activists who have invested in \u003cstrong\u003eMondelez\u003c/strong\u003e, \u003cstrong\u003ePepsi\u003c/strong\u003e, \u003cstrong\u003eP\u0026amp;G \u003c/strong\u003eand \u003cstrong\u003eConAgra\u003c/strong\u003e. Activists have also targeted large companies such as \u003cstrong\u003eHoneywell\u003c/strong\u003e, \u003cstrong\u003eApple \u003c/strong\u003eand \u003cstrong\u003eMicrosoft\u003c/strong\u003e. [The] question with Nestlé is whether this will kick off a round of European consumer activist investments.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eHere is \u003ca href=\"http://www.thirdpointpublic.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/06/Third-Point-Nestle-Letter.pdf\"\u003eLoeb’s letter to investors\u003c/a\u003e, in which he calls on Nestlé to set a formal profit margin target of 18-20 per cent by 2020, boost its debt to buy back shares, put non-core products in its portfolio up for sale and offload that L’Oréal stake. Here’s a \u003ccontent id=\"c47b10ae-5a68-11e7-b553-e2df1b0c3220\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003emore detailed analysis\u003c/content\u003e of the move. Lex’s take: \u003ccontent id=\"49be16e2-5a75-11e7-9bc8-8055f264aa8b\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eit’s worth it\u003c/content\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cimg src=\"https://www.ft.com/__origami/service/image/v2/images/raw/https%3A%2F%2Femail-platform-ftcom-manual.s3-eu-west-1.amazonaws.com%2F480abf55-d036-4d4c-b74b-7c0f7425ce6a?source=ft-email-manual\u0026amp;width=1024\u0026amp;fit=scale-down\" width=\"600\"/\u003e\u003cp\u003e\n\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003ePeople close to the situation tell DD that Loeb’s stake is a constructive position — but he hasn’t ruled out asking for changes to the board. They also said that Loeb and one of his partners met with Nestlé chief executive \u003cstrong\u003eMark Schneider\u003c/strong\u003e — who took over in January — before \u003cstrong\u003eThird Point\u003c/strong\u003e disclosed its position and his reaction to their concerns was “polite”. Loeb is said to want Nestlé to be \"decisive and ambitious\" rather than “glacial” and “slow-moving”, these people said.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eHere’s \u003ccontent id=\"723b4a78-59e8-11e7-b553-e2df1b0c3220\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eour news story\u003c/content\u003e on the move, which sent Nestlé shares to a record — and also sent shares in other European consumer groups higher.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eTake a look at the chart below to see Monday’s movement among those groups — L’Oréal, Danone, Unilever and \u003cstrong\u003eDiageo\u003c/strong\u003e — to get a sense for how Loeb’s letter is reverberating across the sector: investors believe that the activist pressure will spread throughout the industry, forcing the companies to increase profitability targets and take other steps to increase shareholder value.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cimg alt=\"\" src=\"https://www.ft.com/__origami/service/image/v2/images/raw/https%3A%2F%2Femail-platform-ftcom-manual.s3-eu-west-1.amazonaws.com%2F488b1309-4db9-4e5d-9c7e-cad7a7636694?source=ft-email-manual\u0026amp;width=1024\u0026amp;fit=scale-down\" width=\"600\"/\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe herd, it seems, is on the move.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3 class=\"ft-subhead\"\u003eCarlyle’s bounty\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIt took about nine months for \u003cstrong\u003eCarlyle\u003c/strong\u003e, the US buyout group, to find a decent offer for \u003cstrong\u003eHolland \u0026amp; Barrett\u003c/strong\u003e, the health foods retailer. At the eleventh hour, \u003cstrong\u003eMikhail Fridman\u003c/strong\u003e, one of the wealthiest businessmen in Russia, \u003ccontent id=\"5727598e-582b-11e7-9fed-c19e2700005f\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eforked over £1.8bn\u003c/content\u003e, elbowing out keen bidders like \u003cstrong\u003eKKR\u003c/strong\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe lengthy auction process stalled amid the complexities of removing H\u0026amp;B, a retailer, from \u003cstrong\u003eNature’s Bounty\u003c/strong\u003e, a wholesaler. “It’s extremely unusual for a wholesaler to own a retailer so these two businesses didn’t belong together,” said a person involved in the sale.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThere were also costs associated in extracting H\u0026amp;B from shared functions with Nature’s Bounty, including IT services.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe proud new owner will now want to cash in on the growing demand in the health food market but to do so, Fridman will have to push harder on digital offerings and update “old and dusty” stores, people close to the deal told DD.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBut the saga continues. Carlyle wanted from the start to sell the two businesses in one lump. Unable to find a buyer it had to go down the route of selling one to \u003cstrong\u003eL1 Retail\u003c/strong\u003e, a fund set up by Fridman last December.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eDD now understands that a potential sale of Nature’s Bounty — which it acquired seven years ago for $3.8bn, including debt — is on the horizon. Even though an IPO had been on the cards, this was not the case for now, a person familiar with the deal said. In fact, the person said, “there are some discussions going on with potential bidders”.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eCarlyle is expecting “someone to come forward with a compelling offer” but if that was not the case, they are thinking of keeping the businesses rather than listing it, the person added. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e“Transactions aren’t timed perfectly but there is an ongoing effort to sell Nature’s Bounty. The situation is very fluid,” the person said. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eWelcome to your curated briefing on deals and dealmaking by the Financial Times. There were some signs that those restrictions were slowly lifting in the second quarter of the year but the latest campaign has showed that China’s top leaders \u003ccontent id=\"19810ea2-5814-11e7-80b6-9bfa4c1f83d2\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003ewill not tolerate aggressive dealmaking\u003c/content\u003e.\u003cbr/\u003e \u003cbr/\u003eThe FT has a handy explainer of why Anbang is at the centre of the crackdown on leverage. \u003ccontent id=\"526ef434-54d3-11e7-9fed-c19e2700005f\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eYou can read that story here\u003c/content\u003e. DD finds the following to be the most compelling reasons for the attention on the company: \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cp\u003eAnbang is big enough and connected enough to pose some systemic risks: its subsidiaries own Rmb1.06tn ($155bn) worth of shares in mainland-listed companies and has completed foreign acquisitions worth more than $11bn since 2014.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cp\u003eIt has funded some of these acquisitions with high-risk high-return insurance products. Regulators have banned Anbang from selling the products, which could prove dangerous: “This product is like heroin. It’s very dangerous, but you can’t give it up all at once . . . Otherwise, you can generate a significant cash crunch,” said Sam Radwan, partner at Enhance.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cp\u003eSome Chinese publications have said Anbang’s equity base is not what it seems: respected Chinese financial news website \u003cstrong\u003eCaixin\u003c/strong\u003e alleged that the group’s $9bn equity injection in 2014 relied on financial sleight of hand and was “fake”. Anbang has denied the charge and said it intended to sue Caixin. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003ch3 class=\"ft-subhead\"\u003eJob moves\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eCitigroup\u003c/strong\u003e has appointed \u003cstrong\u003eToby Ali\u003c/strong\u003e, who was recently at \u003cstrong\u003eBank of America\u003c/strong\u003e, as co-head of the bank’s leveraged finance business in Europe, the Middle East and Africa. Ali joins \u003cstrong\u003eSimon Francis\u003c/strong\u003e, a former \u003cstrong\u003eCredit Suisse\u003c/strong\u003e executive, who was also appointed co-head of leverage finance for the regions earlier this year. Both Francis and Ali will report to the bank’s head of capital markets, \u003cstrong\u003ePhilip Drury\u003c/strong\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eCiti \u003c/strong\u003ealso hired \u003cstrong\u003eKarl-Georg Altenburg\u003c/strong\u003e as a senior adviser for clients in Emea and Germany. He was co-head of corporate finance for Emea at \u003cstrong\u003eDeutsche Bank\u003c/strong\u003e and was also chief executive and chief country officer for \u003cstrong\u003eJPMorgan\u003c/strong\u003e for Germany, Austria and Switzerland. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eStephen Pierce\u003c/strong\u003e, \u003cstrong\u003eGoldman Sachs\u003c/strong\u003e’s global head of equity capital markets, is retiring after 31 years at the group. Pierce advised on some of the biggest deals of the past three decades — including \u003cstrong\u003eGM\u003c/strong\u003e’s $20bn IPO in 2010, \u003cstrong\u003eSquare\u003c/strong\u003e’s 2015 listing and the US Treasury’s selldown of its stake in \u003cstrong\u003eAIG\u003c/strong\u003e. He also helped take his own company public in 1999. That’s why banks are hiring more cyber security experts to get involved in the dealmaking process.
