{"uuid":"6b526228-5a83-11e7-9bc8-8055f264aa8b","content":{"accessLevel":"subscribed","alternativeStandfirsts":{"promotionalStandfirst":"Emergency support for banks and consumer credit central to BoE update"},"alternativeTitles":{"contentPackageTitle":null,"promotionalTitle":"UK financial stability report: five things to watch"},"body":"\u003cbody\u003e\u003ccontent data-embedded=\"true\" id=\"db728cfe-5a92-11e7-2b35-7545c1789969\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/ImageSet\"\u003e\u003c/content\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis morning at 10.30, the Bank of England will publish its twice-yearly report on UK financial stability. Mark Carney, the bank’s governor, will present the report alongside any recommendations from last week’s Financial Policy Committee meeting.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eQuestions of whether the bank should withdraw its emergency support for banks and clamp down on excess consumer credit are likely to be central features. Here are the main things to look out for:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3 class=\"ft-subhead\"\u003e1. Removing emergency support for banks\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAfter the UK voted for Brexit last June, the FPC worried about the resilience of lending and borrowing and introduced measures it hoped would \u003ca href=\"http://www.bankofengland.co.uk/publications/Documents/fsr/2016/fsrjul16sum.pdf\" title=\"www.bankofengland.co.uk\"\u003eenable banks\u003c/a\u003e to offer up to £150bn of loans to households and companies. The BoE chose to do this by reducing the amount of capital banks had to hold against bad times, allowing them to drop their “counter-cyclical capital buffers” from 0.5 per cent of assets to zero.  \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIt said this emergency rate would stay in place until at least June 2017 and, in March, the FPC said it would make a “full assessment” of whether to raise the buffer to “a more neutral level”. With no shortage of credit, the FPC is poised to increase the buffer, but any change will not be immediate. The FPC normally gives banks a year’s notice of any increase. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe FPC is also likely to tighten the calculation of the liquidity ratio, again reversing action taken immediately after the referendum, ensuring that banks hold more liquid assets as capital in future.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3 class=\"ft-subhead\"\u003e2. Concerns about consumer borrowing\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe BoE has said it is increasingly worried about the growth of consumer credit — \u003ccontent id=\"d340ea28-5040-11e7-bfb8-997009366969\" title=\"www.ft.com\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003ecar loans\u003c/content\u003e, personal loans and credit card debt — which has been rising at annual rates well above 10 per cent. Although consumer debt is much smaller than mortgage debt, the BoE worries that the default rates are likely to be much higher, especially in a world of unsustainably fast consumer credit growth.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIt has already ordered a review of banks’ underwriting standards in consumer credit, particularly the interest-free periods in credit card lending. This meeting is the FPC’s opportunity to damp consumer credit growth. Options include restrictions on low initial “teaser” interest rates on credit cards. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3 class=\"ft-subhead\"\u003e3. Whether car finance is a worry\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBritain has taken to buying \u003ccontent id=\"d340ea28-5040-11e7-bfb8-997009366969\" title=\"www.ft.com\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003enew and used cars on credit\u003c/content\u003e through “personal contract payment” deals that spread the risk of the loss of a car’s value between the consumer and the finance provider. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBut as a glut of new cars sold in the past few years lowers the price of used vehicles, the BoE is worried that troubles in the market could multiply, hurting both finance providers and consumers. Carmakers have also been hit by the fall in the value of the pound, which has raised import prices. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe financial stability report will assess the risks in the car finance market — and whether risks to the stability of the wider financial system have risen as the popularity of car loans has increased. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3 class=\"ft-subhead\"\u003e4. Safe as houses\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe report always assesses risks in the mortgage market, because it is by some margin the most important destination for lending in Britain. The FPC has already limited lenders’ ability to offer high loan to income mortgages and it will report on whether loan books have retained the resilience of the past. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eResidential and commercial mortgage lending growth has been contained in recent months, so the focus is likely to be directed more at the affordability of lending and whether the growing pessimism in the housing market with recent falls in house prices might lead to financial stability risks in the banking sector. The FPC is unlikely to take further action in this area at this meeting. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3 class=\"ft-subhead\"\u003e5. A wary eye on Brexit \u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe BoE prides itself on the relatively smooth operation of financial markets following the EU referendum last year, after which it took emergency action to quell initial panic. The return of calm after last summer’s vote has allowed the BoE to drop its warning that Brexit posed the greatest domestic risk to financial stability.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBut that does not stop the bank from worrying that the Brexit negotiations might lead to a sudden loss of confidence in UK assets, leaving Britain vulnerable because it is dependent, as Mr Carney put it, on the “kindness of strangers”. No action is likely, but expect the BoE to highlight its vigilance about the continued risk. {"uuid":"ec6b4455-6abc-3d1f-a971-927c0dc01074","content":{"accessLevel":"registered","body":"\u003cbody\u003e\u003cp\u003eFor most of the last 20 years, central banking was increasingly a soloist affair: one instrument (interest rates), one target (inflation). Since that didn’t prevent a series of asset price bubbles and gigantic leverage nearly destroying the world economy, fashions have shifted to employing a veritable orchestra of instruments including a “macroprudential controls” section – capital requirements, collateral rules, dynamic loan loss provisioning and so forth. The IMF \u003ca href=\"http://www.imf.org/external/np/sec/pn/2010/pn1089.htm\"\u003ereleased a paper\u003c/a\u003e today which confirms the intellectual shift.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAll very well, but putting this new approach into operation is going to be highly complex, not least because of the potential for normal monetary policy and the new macroprudential roles to get mixed up – one of the reasons that monetary policy and financial supervision were separated in the first place. {"uuid":"e869e23c-528f-11e7-bfb8-997009366969","content":{"accessLevel":"premium","alternativeStandfirsts":{"promotionalStandfirst":"Takeovers are do-able but unlikely"},"alternativeTitles":{"contentPackageTitle":null,"promotionalTitle":"Italian banks: unto the breach"},"body":"\u003cbody\u003e\u003ccontent data-embedded=\"true\" id=\"f91d7eba-53f5-11e7-1ed0-0c60966b289b\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/ImageSet\"\u003e\u003c/content\u003e\u003cp\u003eConventional wisdom insists politicians should only intervene when they can be sure of doing no harm. The result is sometimes inaction with a high cumulative cost. Just ask investors in Italian banks, where \u003ccontent id=\"daafda3c-148e-3b8c-a913-33d8f0e4e79d\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003ethe cost\u003c/content\u003e of doing very little to resolve several troubled regional lenders has weighed on sector valuations for years. \u003cconcept id=\"3f9a60a7-57c8-33ca-aa33-40cf21ea3f51\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/company/PublicCompany\"\u003eSantander\u003c/concept\u003e’s recent \u003ccontent id=\"131c0f3a-4c4d-11e7-a3f4-c742b9791d43\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003epurchase \u003c/content\u003eof Banco Popular suggests an escape route from this torpidity. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eOn paper, there are only two banks in Italy with the firepower \u003ccontent id=\"14f29e5c-4160-11e7-9d56-25f963e998b2\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eto absorb\u003c/content\u003e BP Vicenza and Veneto Banca. \u003cconcept id=\"3d70ab6b-2255-3651-b34f-b5b473f4d669\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/company/PublicCompany\"\u003eUniCredit\u003c/concept\u003e, one possible buyer, is unlikely to be interested after recently completing its own major balance sheet restructuring. That leaves Italy’s strongest big bank — \u003cconcept id=\"5d1d7639-37d9-31dd-b3bf-01baf0713eb6\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/company/PublicCompany\"\u003eIntesa Sanpaolo\u003c/concept\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe prize is obvious: consolidating a position as Italy’s biggest domestic lender by increasing its market share in the country’s wealthy north-east. Given that the formation of non-performing loans is declining to pre-crisis levels, the credit cycle is also supportive. Moreover, a deal is affordable. Assuming that their combined €1bn in subordinated debt is written down, Deutsche Bank reckons ISP would need to raise €5.1bn to buy both banks (for €1 each) while keeping its core tier one equity ratio at current level (about 12.5 per cent). That is less than half UniCredit’s €13bn recent share sale.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe amount needed could fall as long as ISP sells the two banks’ non-performing loans. Around €4bn in additional provisions — roughly the banks’ combined equity — would bring cash coverage of the lenders’ worst loans, the \u003cem\u003esofferenze\u003c/em\u003e, up to 90 per cent — implying a price of 10 cents on the euro — and the rest up to 45 per cent, roughly in line with the sector average.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eA takeover is do-able but unlikely — not least because it would pile pressure on ISP’s pledge to pay a rising dividend. After years of economic trouble, there are concerns about damage to the franchise value in both banks. This is why ISP (and UniCredit) are likely to invest much smaller sums in the banks as part of a state-backed “\u003ccontent id=\"03048068-5031-11e7-a1f2-db19572361bb\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eprecautionary recapitalisation\u003c/content\u003e”. {"uuid":"e869e23c-528f-11e7-bfb8-997009366969","content":{"accessLevel":"premium","alternativeStandfirsts":{"promotionalStandfirst":"Takeovers are do-able but unlikely"},"alternativeTitles":{"contentPackageTitle":null,"promotionalTitle":"Italian banks: unto the breach"},"body":"\u003cbody\u003e\u003ccontent data-embedded=\"true\" id=\"f91d7eba-53f5-11e7-1ed0-0c60966b289b\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/ImageSet\"\u003e\u003c/content\u003e\u003cp\u003eConventional wisdom insists politicians should only intervene when they can be sure of doing no harm. The result is sometimes inaction with a high cumulative cost. Just ask investors in Italian banks, where \u003ccontent id=\"daafda3c-148e-3b8c-a913-33d8f0e4e79d\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003ethe cost\u003c/content\u003e of doing very little to resolve several troubled regional lenders has weighed on sector valuations for years. \u003cconcept id=\"3f9a60a7-57c8-33ca-aa33-40cf21ea3f51\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/company/PublicCompany\"\u003eSantander\u003c/concept\u003e’s recent \u003ccontent id=\"131c0f3a-4c4d-11e7-a3f4-c742b9791d43\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003epurchase \u003c/content\u003eof Banco Popular suggests an escape route from this torpidity. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eOn paper, there are only two banks in Italy with the firepower \u003ccontent id=\"14f29e5c-4160-11e7-9d56-25f963e998b2\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eto absorb\u003c/content\u003e BP Vicenza and Veneto Banca. \u003cconcept id=\"3d70ab6b-2255-3651-b34f-b5b473f4d669\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/company/PublicCompany\"\u003eUniCredit\u003c/concept\u003e, one possible buyer, is unlikely to be interested after recently completing its own major balance sheet restructuring. That leaves Italy’s strongest big bank — \u003cconcept id=\"5d1d7639-37d9-31dd-b3bf-01baf0713eb6\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/company/PublicCompany\"\u003eIntesa Sanpaolo\u003c/concept\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe prize is obvious: consolidating a position as Italy’s biggest domestic lender by increasing its market share in the country’s wealthy north-east. Given that the formation of non-performing loans is declining to pre-crisis levels, the credit cycle is also supportive. Moreover, a deal is affordable. Assuming that their combined €1bn in subordinated debt is written down, Deutsche Bank reckons ISP would need to raise €5.1bn to buy both banks (for €1 each) while keeping its core tier one equity ratio at current level (about 12.5 per cent). That is less than half UniCredit’s €13bn recent share sale.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe amount needed could fall as long as ISP sells the two banks’ non-performing loans. Around €4bn in additional provisions — roughly the banks’ combined equity — would bring cash coverage of the lenders’ worst loans, the \u003cem\u003esofferenze\u003c/em\u003e, up to 90 per cent — implying a price of 10 cents on the euro — and the rest up to 45 per cent, roughly in line with the sector average.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eA takeover is do-able but unlikely — not least because it would pile pressure on ISP’s pledge to pay a rising dividend. After years of economic trouble, there are concerns about damage to the franchise value in both banks. This is why ISP (and UniCredit) are likely to invest much smaller sums in the banks as part of a state-backed “\u003ccontent id=\"03048068-5031-11e7-a1f2-db19572361bb\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eprecautionary recapitalisation\u003c/content\u003e”. Without consolidation, however, it is unlikely to be the kind of decisive action Italy’s banking sector needs.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eEmail the Lex team at \u003ca {"uuid":"18281550-56ac-11e7-80b6-9bfa4c1f83d2","content":{"accessLevel":"subscribed","alternativeStandfirsts":{"promotionalStandfirst":"Trusty gauge usually predicts US economic downturns but there are some positive signs"},"alternativeTitles":{"contentPackageTitle":null,"promotionalTitle":"Investor nervousness rises as yield curve flattens"},"body":"\u003cbody\u003e\u003ccontent data-embedded=\"true\" id=\"ff43c2b2-5a4f-11e7-2b35-7545c1789969\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/ImageSet\"\u003e\u003c/content\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe leading market indicator of economic distress is flashing orange again, challenging the US Federal Reserve’s insistence on \u003ccontent id=\"19078a18-5144-11e7-bfb8-997009366969\" title=\"Investors wary of Fed’s newfound assertiveness - FT\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eraising interest rates \u003c/content\u003eand the stock market’s blithe march to record highs. But could this usually trusty gauge be faulty this time?\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eInvestors are, for the most part, as poor at forecasting recessions as professional economists, often climbing higher even as the ground quakes beneath their feet. But the so-called “yield curve” has predicted almost every US economic downturn since the second world war and is now blinking an ominous colour.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe yield curve is the slope shaped by the yields of government bonds of various maturities. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eTypically it costs more to issue long-term bonds than shorter term ones, so the curve slopes upwards most of the time. But when the yield curve flattens and eventually inverts, it tends to presage a recession. And the \u003ccontent id=\"0bdbe45c-d0fc-11e5-831d-09f7778e7377\" title=\"Yield curve recession indicator sends warning on US economy - FT\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eUS yield curve\u003c/content\u003e has dramatically flattened this year, sparking concern among some investors.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e“It’s been a pretty powerful indicator in the past,” said Gregory Peters, senior investment officer at PGIM Fixed Income. “It’s still a very instructive gauge and you have to pay attention to it.” \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe yield curve initially steepened heavily in the wake of last year’s presidential election, as investors grew more hopeful that President Donald Trump’s promised economic policies could improve economic growth and lift inflation. But, for most of 2017, it has deflated like a punctured balloon.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe widely watched spread between two and 30-year Treasury yields — one of the best measures of the yield curve — fell to a 10-year low of just 137 basis points this week, down from more than 200bp towards the end of last year. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ccontent data-embedded=\"true\" id=\"69d9cca6-5812-11e7-1ed0-0c60966b289b\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/ImageSet\"\u003e\u003c/content\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe difference between two and 10-year Treasury yields, another popular measure, has fallen to 80bp, close to the nine-year low of 75bp touched last summer.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe yield curve has been flattening as a result of the Federal Reserve raising interest rates, which pushes up the policy-sensitive two-year Treasury yield. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMeanwhile, longer term government bond yields have fallen, reflecting ebbing expectations for growth and inflation, with the latter reinforced of late by weakening oil prices. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe \u003ccontent id=\"8bb044d0-ff76-11e6-8d8e-a5e3738f9ae4\" title=\"Bond investors send warning for record high equity market - FT\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eeconomic pessimism\u003c/content\u003e signalled by long-term Treasury yields stands in sharp contrast to the ebullience of the US stock market, which hit a record high this week. Some strategists say this divergence cannot last. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003crelated id=\"8bb044d0-ff76-11e6-8d8e-a5e3738f9ae4\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003e\u003ctitle\u003eInvesting under Trump\u003c/title\u003e\u003cheadline\u003eBond investors send warning for record high equity market\n\u003c/headline\u003e\u003cmedia\u003e\u003ccontent data-embedded=\"true\" id=\"0e5b9930-0007-11e7-13e8-3279a9fb31ad\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/ImageSet\"\u003e\u003c/content\u003e\u003c/media\u003e\u003cintro\u003e\u003cp\u003eStructural reasons that have kept yields low seen staying in place\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/intro\u003e\u003c/related\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBank of America analysts recently estimated that Treasury yields either had to rise 50-65 basis points or stocks fall 13-20 per cent to reconcile the differing growth outlooks baked into their prices. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThey think a bond sell-off is the most likely outcome but fixed income investors tend to have a better grasp of economic fundamentals than equity investors.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eNonetheless, while there are reasons to be nervous about what the yield curve is whispering, many investors point out that there are also grounds for optimism. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eLonger term bond yields are also pressured by global flows of money that the Fed can do little about and which have little to do with the US economic outlook. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eChina has resumed buying Treasuries and the European Central Bank and Bank of Japan still have their foot to the monetary pedal. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThis keeps Japanese and eurozone bond yields near record lows and spurs many investors, desperate for some yield in still-safe bonds, into the US government market, muffling any signal the yield curve sends. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e“We still have these yield refugees from Japan and the eurozone,” said Jim Leaviss, head of retail fixed income at M\u0026amp;G Investments. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eHe argues that the flattening of the yield curve might even be an encouraging development for the Fed. This reflects how wary the central bank’s policymakers are of upsetting the bond market after the turbulence that followed the tapering of its quantitative easing programme, which caused mortgage costs — which are benchmarked to Treasuries — to shoot higher and \u003ca href=\"https://www.stlouisfed.org/publications/housing-market-perspectives/issue-4-march-2017/could-housing-markets-face-another-taper-tantrum-moment\" title=\"Could Housing Markets Face Another \u0026quot;Taper Tantrum\u0026quot; Moment? - St Louis Fed\"\u003ethreatened to derail \u003c/a\u003ethe housing recovery.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e“If I was the Fed, I’d be happy with this rather than unhappy,” Mr Leaviss said. “US mortgage rates are tied to the long end of the bond market and getting interest rates back to normal without startling the long end is positive.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eLarry Hatheway, chief economist and fund manager at GAM, an asset manager, points out that the yield curve is still far from inverting and argues that the recent flattening has been primarily driven by falling inflation and expectations of inflation. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eSo-called “break-even” rates — a measure of investors’ inflation expectations derived from comparing the yields of inflation-protected and conventional government bonds — have slid precipitously this year. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ccontent data-embedded=\"true\" id=\"6c8bf4ba-5812-11e7-1ed0-0c60966b289b\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/ImageSet\"\u003e\u003c/content\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhile there is clearly “nervousness” in markets about the outlook, the decline in inflation expectations has been a bigger factor in the yield curve deflation, Mr Hatheway argues. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e“There’s been a pretty significant adjustment in break-evens,” he said. “If people were really ratcheting back growth expectations, we should have seen a wider manifestation of that in markets.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eStill, those nerves are not going away — and some investors are taking money off the table as a result. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eTake Phase Capital, a computer-driven hedge fund that uses a wide variety of market indicators for its models that calculate how much risk it should take. The flattening yield curve has led it to roughly halve its market exposure, said Michael DePalma, Phase Capital chief executive. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e“We have found the yield curve to be a persistently reliable indicator,” he said. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ccontent data-embedded=\"true\" id=\"38f3c9d6-72a8-4b43-a646-6e2bfc016d6b\" {"uuid":"ddb2e36f-7e70-3ca1-a81c-de9bdcfc4441","content":{"accessLevel":"subscribed","body":"\u003cbody\u003e\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ca href=\"http://blogs.ft.com/material-world/files/2014/04/Alex-Wang.jpg\"\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"396\" src=\"http://blogs.ft.com/material-world/files/2014/04/Alex-Wang-272x396.jpg\" width=\"272\"/\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eSo Alexander Wang, left, is the latest runway designer to team up with \u003ca href=\"http://www.ft.com/topics/organisations/H\u0026amp;M_Hennes_\u0026amp;_Mauritz_AB\"\u003eH\u0026amp;M\u003c/a\u003e in their high/low limited-edition strategy for creating buzz and best-sellers. He’ll be the first American to get the gig. The news was announced on Sunday by Mr Wang via Instagram, which was seen as very cool, while at Coachella, which is even more cool. The message being, of course, that he is just cool, and this project is going to be super-cool.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eExcept it always seems to me the appeal of the H\u0026amp;M collaborations was they took names that weren’t cool – they were haute, and generally unreachable – and it was the combination of unlikely bedfellows (the high street and the high fashion) that was actually the cool part. This one seems to indicate a change in strategy.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eI mean, think about the names that preceded Mr Wang most successfully: Alber Elbaz of Lanvin; Karl Lagerfeld; Jimmy Choo; Comme des Garçons; Versace; Marni; Stella McCartney; Maison Martin Margiela. All of them seen as very inaccessible, either because of their prices or their aesthetics, to most consumers. Even Isabel Marant, who has made a name for herself on the upper edges of the French contemporary market, was relatively inaccessible to most shoppers outside of that country, and hence had an edge of get-it-quick-while-you-can.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMr Wang, however, both because of his very successful and more accessible \u003ca href=\"http://www.alexanderwang.com/shop/womens//t-by-alexander-wang/all/\"\u003eT by Alexander Wang\u003c/a\u003e line, and his very smiley and outgoing personality, is much less of an unknown and elitist quantity. Sure, for the past three seasons he has designed for \u003ca href=\"http://blogs.ft.com/material-world/2012/12/03/wang-officially-moves-to-balenciaga-more-than-doubles-workload/\"\u003eBalenciaga\u003c/a\u003e, but that’s not really enough to have established him in the thin-air regions of fashion; he may well get there, but it has not happened yet. And anyway, this isn’t Balenciaga for H\u0026amp;M, it’s Wang – which is an important difference.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eHe’s a more populist choice, both in terms of name recognition and aesthetic, than those that came before. He has less mystique – though it is not to say his collection won’t sell very well.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eA tee at T, for example, still costs about $100, and a cotton-ribbed dress is $450, so presumably whatever he does for H\u0026amp;M will be cheaper, which means the Wang-ettes will be waiting. Plus, it also positions Mr Wang – between Balenciaga, his own line, and T – as the Karl Lagerfeld (aka the most polymorphous designer) of his generation. Between that, and the marketing muscle that comes with H\u0026amp;M, you can see the definite upside for him.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFor H\u0026amp;M, though, while Mr Wang’s aura may give them a new veneer of buzz, it may not do what some of their other collaborations have done, which is entice entirely new consumers, who are drawn by the chance to get a piece of a couture brand, through their doors.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eUltimately, it feels less like a chance to buy the dream than an opportunity to enter an already open door at a lower price point. Will it work as well?\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/body\u003e","brands":[{"id":"http://api.ft.com/things/13006c72-7d1b-47a0-96fe-d1ad1f12de9f"},{"id":"http://api.ft.com/things/dbb0bdae-1f0c-11e4-b0cb-b2227cce2b54"}],"byline":"Vanessa Friedman","canBeDistributed":"yes","canBeSyndicated":"verify","comments":{"enabled":true},"description":null,"externalBinaryUrl":null,"firstPublishedDate":"2014-04-14T11:18:37.000Z","identifiers":[{"authority":"http://api.ft.com/system/FT-LABS-WP-1-106","identifierValue":"http://blogs.ft.com/material-world/2014/04/14/assessing-the-alexander-wanghm-collaboration/"},{"authority":"http://api.ft.com/system/FT-LABS-WP-1-106","identifierValue":"http://blogs.ft.com/material-world/?p=90912"}],"internalBinaryUrl":null,"lastModified":"2018-04-04T12:58:49.033Z","mainImage":null,"mediaType":null,"members":null,"opening":"\u003cbody\u003e\u003cp\u003eSo Alexander Wang, left, is the latest runway designer to team up with H\u0026amp;M in their high/low limited-edition strategy for creating buzz and best-sellers. {"uuid":"ec6b4455-6abc-3d1f-a971-927c0dc01074","content":{"accessLevel":"registered","body":"\u003cbody\u003e\u003cp\u003eFor most of the last 20 years, central banking was increasingly a soloist affair: one instrument (interest rates), one target (inflation). Since that didn’t prevent a series of asset price bubbles and gigantic leverage nearly destroying the world economy, fashions have shifted to employing a veritable orchestra of instruments including a “macroprudential controls” section – capital requirements, collateral rules, dynamic loan loss provisioning and so forth. The IMF \u003ca href=\"http://www.imf.org/external/np/sec/pn/2010/pn1089.htm\"\u003ereleased a paper\u003c/a\u003e today which confirms the intellectual shift.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAll very well, but putting this new approach into operation is going to be highly complex, not least because of the potential for normal monetary policy and the new macroprudential roles to get mixed up – one of the reasons that monetary policy and financial supervision were separated in the first place. Central banks are going to have to learn how to be independent of themselves.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/body\u003e","brands":[{"id":"http://api.ft.com/things/dbb0bdae-1f0c-11e4-b0cb-b2227cce2b54"},{"id":"http://api.ft.com/things/e6bfdc7d-6967-3b7b-affe-935f4618174d"}],"byline":"Alan Beattie","canBeDistributed":"yes","canBeSyndicated":"verify","comments":{"enabled":true},"description":null,"externalBinaryUrl":null,"firstPublishedDate":"2010-07-20T21:30:33.000Z","identifiers":[{"authority":"http://api.ft.com/system/FT-LABS-WP-1-56","identifierValue":"http://blogs.ft.com/money-supply/2010/07/20/the-imfs-new-central-banking-orchestra/"},{"authority":"http://api.ft.com/system/FT-LABS-WP-1-56","identifierValue":"http://blogs.ft.com/money-supply/?p=59301"}],"internalBinaryUrl":null,"lastModified":"2018-04-04T12:58:50.594Z","mainImage":null,"mediaType":null,"members":null,"opening":"\u003cbody\u003e\u003cp\u003eFor most of the last 20 years, central banking was increasingly a soloist affair: one instrument (interest rates), one target (inflation). Since that didn’t prevent a series of asset price bubbles and gigantic leverage nearly destroying the world economy, fashions have shifted to employing a veritable orchestra of instruments including a “macroprudential controls” section – capital requirements, collateral rules, dynamic loan loss provisioning and so forth. The IMF \u003ca href=\"http://www.imf.org/external/np/sec/pn/2010/pn1089.htm\"\u003ereleased a paper\u003c/a\u003e today which confirms the intellectual shift.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAll very well, but putting this new approach into operation is going to be highly complex, not least because of the potential for normal monetary policy and the new macroprudential roles to get mixed up – one of the reasons that monetary policy and financial supervision were separated in the first place. Central banks are going to have to learn how to be independent of themselves.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/body\u003e","pixelHeight":null,"pixelWidth":null,"publishReference":"SYNTHETIC-REQ-MON_tid_wp-money-supply_ec6b4455-6abc-3d1f-a971-927c0dc01074_carousel_1522846719","publishedDate":"2010-07-20T21:30:33.000Z","standout":{"editorsChoice":false,"exclusive":false,"scoop":false},"title":"The IMF’s new central banking orchestra","titles":null,"type":"Article","uuid":"ec6b4455-6abc-3d1f-a971-927c0dc01074","webUrl":"http://blogs.ft.com/money-supply/2010/07/20/the-imfs-new-central-banking-orchestra/"},"metadata":[{"thing":{"id":"http://api.ft.com/things/bc090d1d-77de-3a94-9919-aade7e1c7333","prefLabel":"International Monetary Fund","types":["http://www.ft.com/ontology/core/Thing","http://www.ft.com/ontology/concept/Concept","http://www.ft.com/ontology/organisation/Organisation"],"predicate":"http://www.ft.com/ontology/annotation/mentions","apiUrl":"http://api.ft.com/organisations/bc090d1d-77de-3a94-9919-aade7e1c7333"}}],"contentUri":"http://wordpress-article-mapper.svc.ft.com/content/ec6b4455-6abc-3d1f-a971-927c0dc01074","lastModified":"2018-04-04T12:58:50.594Z","markedDeleted":"false"}

{"uuid":"58d29c29-58a0-34b3-b5fd-0526789d8caf","content":{"accessLevel":"registered","body":"\u003cbody\u003e\u003cp\u003eSir Alan Budd, interim chairman of the \u003ca href=\"http://blogs.ft.com/money-supply/2010/05/17/the-obrs-flaws-and-benefits/\"\u003eOffice for Budget Responsibility\u003c/a\u003e, was on the money this morning in highlighting what a difficult job his successor faces. He said:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cblockquote\u003e\u003cp\u003e“No one in their right mind would take on a job in which your success is judged by your success in fiscal forecasts”.\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/blockquote\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe OBR is not alone in this plight. Members of the Monetary Policy Committee take interest rate decisions which are predicated on the Bank of England’s forecasts. But in having a decision as well as a forecast, the MPC is always able to claim that it took the right decision based on the information available at the time, which takes the focus away from its often poor forecasts.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eSo much for the communication surrounding errors. The origination of forecast errors is more important. And the OBR’s greater, though far from perfect, transparency shows that its Budget forecast hinges on very thin and quite weird stuff.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe most important number in the OBR’s forecast is its estimate of the output gap – the difference between the economy’s measured output and an estimate of the economy’s potential output.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIt is crucial because it determines the OBR’s estimate of the structural deficit and hence governs the Budget judgement. Deciding the output gap was 4 per cent at the end of 2009 allowed the chancellor to say the underlying public finances were worse than previously published even though the actual budget deficit was better than previous Treasury forecasts.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn a modification of past Treasury practice, the OBR decided the output gap assumption should not be subject to revision when the data changes. It justified its conclusion that the output gap was 4 per cent of GDP at the end of 2009 with the following chart, which showed a bunch of undefined indicators were around 4 per cent below their normal levels at the end of 2009.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"410\" src=\"http://blogs.ft.com/money-supply/files/2010/07/20-july-2010-output-gap-by-obr1-590x410.jpg\" width=\"590\"/\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAssuming the chart’s final data point is  2010  Q1 (I have asked the OBR repeatedly, but they have not got back to me), the 4 per cent output gap assumption follows from this chart, as the arrow shows.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eSo much, so good. But if you are basing the most important assumption of your forecast on this chart, you might look to some other bits of if you want to satisfy yourself that the 4 per cent figure for the end of 2009 is OK. There is nothing reassuring about the rest of the chart.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"410\" src=\"http://blogs.ft.com/money-supply/files/2010/07/20-july-2010-obr-output-gap-590x410.jpg\" width=\"590\"/\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAs the oval in 2009 shows, the method which gives the 4 per cent figure for the output gap also suggests the output gap shrank in 2009 from about 5.2 per cent in the second quarter to that 4 per cent in the fourth quarter. And that the output gap was bigger in the fourth quarter of 2008 than in the fourth quarter of 2009. That is weird.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIt implies that the economy grew faster than trend in the year to Q4 2009. This was a year in which the economy shrank by 2.9 per cent. Between the second quarter of 2009 and the fourth quarter of 2009, the economy did grow by 0.2 per cent, but this modest growth alongside a big fall in the output gap, implies a trend rate of growth of about -2 per cent. Do we really believe that trend growth is normally 2.3 per cent but between the second and fourth quarter of 2009 it was -2 per cent?\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eCertainly, the Treasury’s former method of working out the output gap does not suggest anything of the sort. It has the output gap growing significantly in 2009 as the following 2010 emergency Budget chart shows (on either trend growth assumptions).\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"473\" src=\"http://blogs.ft.com/money-supply/files/2010/07/22-june-new-economy-picture-590x473.jpg\" width=\"590\"/\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe upshot is that the OBR is likely to have to make big and public changes to its output gap assumptions in future forecasts. If it decides the output gap is bigger, the Treasury will suddenly seem awash with funds and it will raise further questions about the OBR’s independence.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIf the output gap is deemed to close, the chancellor will come under pressure to cut spending further or raise taxes. And this pressure would come all because of a chart in which the x-axis is difficult to read, makes little sense as a time series and in which  the underlying analysis has not been released by the OBR.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIt all seems a rather flimsy basis for setting fiscal policy. But that is the real hospital pass the poor OBR has been given because it is forced to make specific calls on the structural deficit which depend on an unknowable assumption about the output gap. {"uuid":"4e6baa29-b63a-3cfa-98bb-72a3da684b96","content":{"accessLevel":"registered","body":"\u003cbody\u003e\u003cp\u003eAn \u003ca href=\"http://www.federalreserve.gov/pubs/ifdp/2010/1001/ifdp1001.pdf\"\u003einteresting research paper\u003c/a\u003e from the Federal Reserve staff has drawn my attention to a \u003ca href=\"http://www.federalreserve.gov/newsevents/speech/kohn20091009a.htm\"\u003espeech Fed deputy chair Don Kohn made last October\u003c/a\u003e about why the Fed has not promised to keep rates low enough for long enough to allow a bit of inflation in the future (such as by setting a price level target).\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cblockquote\u003e\u003cp\u003eTo be sure, we have not followed the theoretical prescription of promising to keep rates low enough for long enough to create a period of above-normal inflation. The arguments in favor of such a policy hinge on a clear understanding on the part of the public that the central bank will tolerate increased inflation only temporarily–say, for a few years once the economy has recovered–before returning to the original inflation target in the long term. In standard theoretical model environments, long-run inflation expectations are perfectly anchored. In reality, however, the anchoring of inflation expectations has been a hard-won achievement of monetary policy over the past few decades, and we should not take this stability for granted. Models are by their nature only a stylized representation of reality, and a policy of achieving “temporarily” higher inflation over the medium term would run the risk of altering inflation expectations beyond the horizon that is desirable. Were that to happen, the costs of bringing expectations back to their current anchored state might be quite high.\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/blockquote\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn essence, any promise to allow a bit of inflation in the future, even if it were the best way to escape from deflation now, comes with risks to the central bank’s credibility. A policy of this sort is \u003ca href=\"http://blogs.ft.com/money-supply/2010/07/19/easing-and-fed-communications/\"\u003eone option for the Fed\u003c/a\u003e if the economy deteriorates a fair bit further and it decides that it needs to ease. At what point would the risk of deflation outweigh the risk to the Fed’s credibility?\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/body\u003e","brands":[{"id":"http://api.ft.com/things/dbb0bdae-1f0c-11e4-b0cb-b2227cce2b54"},{"id":"http://api.ft.com/things/e6bfdc7d-6967-3b7b-affe-935f4618174d"}],"byline":"Robin Harding","canBeDistributed":"yes","canBeSyndicated":"verify","comments":{"enabled":true},"description":null,"externalBinaryUrl":null,"firstPublishedDate":"2010-07-20T14:41:12.000Z","identifiers":[{"authority":"http://api.ft.com/system/FT-LABS-WP-1-56","identifierValue":"http://blogs.ft.com/money-supply/2010/07/20/more-about-price-level-targets-and-the-fed/"},{"authority":"http://api.ft.com/system/FT-LABS-WP-1-56","identifierValue":"http://blogs.ft.com/money-supply/?p=59241"}],"internalBinaryUrl":null,"lastModified":"2018-04-04T12:58:51.996Z","mainImage":null,"mediaType":null,"members":null,"opening":"\u003cbody\u003e\u003cp\u003eAn \u003ca href=\"http://www.federalreserve.gov/pubs/ifdp/2010/1001/ifdp1001.pdf\"\u003einteresting research paper\u003c/a\u003e from the Federal Reserve staff has drawn my attention to a \u003ca href=\"http://www.federalreserve.gov/newsevents/speech/kohn20091009a.htm\"\u003espeech Fed deputy chair Don Kohn made last October\u003c/a\u003e about why the Fed has not promised to keep rates low enough for long enough to allow a bit of inflation in the future (such as by setting a price level target).\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cblockquote\u003e\u003cp\u003eTo be sure, we have not followed the theoretical prescription of promising to keep rates low enough for long enough to create a period of above-normal inflation. {"uuid":"bbc9ff5b-916a-3eb0-a379-975922936c25","content":{"accessLevel":"subscribed","body":"\u003cbody\u003e\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ca href=\"http://blogs.ft.com/material-world/files/2014/04/Hugo-Boss1.jpg\"\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"391\" src=\"http://blogs.ft.com/material-world/files/2014/04/Hugo-Boss1-272x391.jpg\" width=\"272\"/\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBig (literally) news on Monday in the FT that on Sunday, thanks to a government recalculation, \u003ca href=\"http://www.ft.com/cms/s/0/70b594fe-bd94-11e3-a5ba-00144feabdc0.html\"\u003eNigeria’s GDP\u003c/a\u003e has now become the biggest in Africa, and the 26th biggest in the world, valued at $509bn. Why do we (we luxury folks, that is) care?\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e“The revision will have a psychological impact. It underlines to foreign investors that this country has a large consumer base. It validates the investment thesis,” said Ngozi Okonjo-Iweala, the minister for economy and finance.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eSo will luxury, which has thus far been dancing around the edges of the country (only Zegna and Hugo Boss have standalone stores in Lagos, opened last year, and Diesel recently joined them), but which these days really loves a new consumer base, rush in?\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eI have to say, I doubt it – at least in the near term. I think they may look more carefully at the country, and perhaps even explore options, but my guess is they will remain wary, at least when it comes to the kinds of brands that line the Avenues Montaigne and Montenapoleone. Here are some reasons:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFirst, there are the logistical challenges, which are meaningful. Second, last winter I was having a conversation with the CEO of an enormously successful international accessory brand that will remain nameless, and the subject of \u003ca href=\"http://www.ft.com/topics/places/Nigeria\"\u003eNigeria\u003c/a\u003e came up, but when I asked about it, he shook his head. Aside from systemic factors, he said he just didn’t feel comfortable with where a lot of the money was coming from. It was partly, he said, a moral decision.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ca href=\"http://blogs.ft.com/material-world/files/2014/04/chanel-globe.jpg\"\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"180\" src=\"http://blogs.ft.com/material-world/files/2014/04/chanel-globe-272x180.jpg\" width=\"272\"/\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eNow, it would be completely fair to roll your eyes at this and point out morals didn’t stop luxury from running headlong into Russia or any number of other countries (including the US, back in the days when the robber barons ruled the economy); that often in history the cycle of wealth goes from the more oblique to the acceptable in origin, and luxury is, in fact, a crucial part of that cycle, but nevertheless, I believed him. Which was interesting. And I don’t think he’s the only one with such concerns, as the globe (above) at the Chanel show in March 2013 illustrating all its stores around the world via little lights, demonstrated: Africa was, in their terms, the dark continent.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eLuxury is, after all, obsessed with its image. And this is, in some ways, a question of image.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eOn the other hand, that GDP number is a big figure. And the population of Nigeria – 169m – is also a big number. So it will be interesting to see which impulse wins out: the rush to numbers or the protection of image.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThere is clearly a struggle going on, as we have been trying to get an executive to join our \u003ca href=\"http://www.ftbusinessofluxury.com/ehome/index.php?eventid=75766\u0026amp;\"\u003eBusiness of Luxury summit\u003c/a\u003e, which is taking place next month in Mexico City and will look at markets of the future (I encourage everyone to sign up and come – it’s going to be great), to talk specifically about this issue and guess what: they are all scared. No one wants to commit publicly, because, I venture to guess, they can’t quite decide.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eOf course, luxury also has that safety-in-number thing – as consultant Robert Burke once told me, for an industry obsessed with creativity, it is remarkably “conservative” – so if a few more stores enter the market the whole thing will probably tip, and they will all start to look for space. In the end, competitive instinct may be the deciding factor.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn the meantime, however, anyone else have any thoughts? Maybe Paddy Power should start giving odds.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/body\u003e","brands":[{"id":"http://api.ft.com/things/13006c72-7d1b-47a0-96fe-d1ad1f12de9f"},{"id":"http://api.ft.com/things/dbb0bdae-1f0c-11e4-b0cb-b2227cce2b54"}],"byline":"Vanessa Friedman","canBeDistributed":"yes","canBeSyndicated":"verify","comments":{"enabled":true},"description":null,"externalBinaryUrl":null,"firstPublishedDate":"2014-04-07T11:34:53.000Z","identifiers":[{"authority":"http://api.ft.com/system/FT-LABS-WP-1-106","identifierValue":"http://blogs.ft.com/material-world/2014/04/07/will-luxury-rush-to-nigeria-now/"},{"authority":"http://api.ft.com/system/FT-LABS-WP-1-106","identifierValue":"http://blogs.ft.com/material-world/?p=90292"}],"internalBinaryUrl":null,"lastModified":"2018-04-04T12:58:55.031Z","mainImage":null,"mediaType":null,"members":null,"opening":"\u003cbody\u003e\u003cp\u003eBig (literally) news today in the FT that yesterday, thanks to a government recalculation, Nigeria’s GDP has not become the biggest in Africa, and the 26th biggest in the world, valued at $509bn. {"uuid":"4e6baa29-b63a-3cfa-98bb-72a3da684b96","content":{"accessLevel":"registered","body":"\u003cbody\u003e\u003cp\u003eAn \u003ca href=\"http://www.federalreserve.gov/pubs/ifdp/2010/1001/ifdp1001.pdf\"\u003einteresting research paper\u003c/a\u003e from the Federal Reserve staff has drawn my attention to a \u003ca href=\"http://www.federalreserve.gov/newsevents/speech/kohn20091009a.htm\"\u003espeech Fed deputy chair Don Kohn made last October\u003c/a\u003e about why the Fed has not promised to keep rates low enough for long enough to allow a bit of inflation in the future (such as by setting a price level target).\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cblockquote\u003e\u003cp\u003eTo be sure, we have not followed the theoretical prescription of promising to keep rates low enough for long enough to create a period of above-normal inflation. The arguments in favor of such a policy hinge on a clear understanding on the part of the public that the central bank will tolerate increased inflation only temporarily–say, for a few years once the economy has recovered–before returning to the original inflation target in the long term. In standard theoretical model environments, long-run inflation expectations are perfectly anchored. In reality, however, the anchoring of inflation expectations has been a hard-won achievement of monetary policy over the past few decades, and we should not take this stability for granted. Models are by their nature only a stylized representation of reality, and a policy of achieving “temporarily” higher inflation over the medium term would run the risk of altering inflation expectations beyond the horizon that is desirable. Were that to happen, the costs of bringing expectations back to their current anchored state might be quite high.\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/blockquote\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn essence, any promise to allow a bit of inflation in the future, even if it were the best way to escape from deflation now, comes with risks to the central bank’s credibility. A policy of this sort is \u003ca href=\"http://blogs.ft.com/money-supply/2010/07/19/easing-and-fed-communications/\"\u003eone option for the Fed\u003c/a\u003e if the economy deteriorates a fair bit further and it decides that it needs to ease. This is partly because of the behind-the-scenes psychodrama surrounding his appointment, and the Montague and Capulet relationship between LVMH and his former employer, Kering.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003ePersonally, however, what fascinates me is whether he will be able to pull off a credible ready-to-wear collection in sales terms. Vuitton always treated former artistic director Marc Jacobs’ collections as more show pieces, and less commercial endeavours, and they were only sold in a few select LV stores. So while he transformed Vuitton into a fashion brand image-wise, it wasn’t really a fashion brand revenue-wise. Now word is strategic importance is being placed on the clothes. Let’s see what that means.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ca href=\"http://blogs.ft.com/material-world/files/2014/02/JW-Anderson-award1.jpg\"\u003e\u003cimg alt=\"\" height=\"398\" src=\"http://blogs.ft.com/material-world/files/2014/02/JW-Anderson-award1-272x398.jpg\" width=\"272\"/\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e2. \u003ca href=\"http://blogs.ft.com/material-world/2013/09/26/the-jw-anderson-loewe-deal-and-the-shifting-balance-of-power/\"\u003eJonathan Anderson\u003c/a\u003e’s debut for Loewe. When gender-bending YBD Jonathan Anderson (aka JW Anderson, left) was named creative director of Loewe, following Stuart Vevers, the fashion world was surprised, Loewe being touted as the “Hermes” (read: conservative high-luxury brand) of Spain, and Mr Anderson being something of an agent provocateur in Britain. It also marked Delphine Arnault’s first big young designer move. This will be the first test of whether it was a smart move, or one that generated a lot of sound and fury and maybe confused consumers.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e3. What’s afoot at Vionnet. Hussein Chalayan has already done one demi-\u003ca href=\"http://www.ft.com/cms/s/2/5b724f08-8412-11e3-9903-00144feab7de.html#slide0\"\u003ecouture show\u003c/a\u003e for the brand that was a little questionable, but he had only been in place for a few weeks, and there’s no doubting his extraordinary talent. Now it will be interesting to see how that collection relates to the ready-to-wear offerings from owner and former designer \u003ca href=\"http://www.ft.com/cms/s/2/b011a812-d8da-11e2-84fa-00144feab7de.html\"\u003eGoga Ashkenazi\u003c/a\u003e later this week – if at all. Can it really be possible for a house to have such very different designers for different lines, and still create a consistent and complimentary brand aesthetic? We’ll soon find out.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003ca href=\"http://www.ft.com/intl/cms/s/0/6c77adb2-dddb-11dd-87dc-000077b07658.html\"\u003eHussein Chalayan\u003c/a\u003e’s debut for Vionnet. OK, he did already have one \u003ca href=\"http://www.ft.com/cms/s/2/5b724f08-8412-11e3-9903-00144feab7de.html#slide0\"\u003ecouture show\u003c/a\u003e for the brand that was a little questionable, but he had only been in place for a few weeks, and there’s no doubting his extraordinary talent. {"uuid":"18281550-56ac-11e7-80b6-9bfa4c1f83d2","content":{"accessLevel":"subscribed","alternativeStandfirsts":{"promotionalStandfirst":"Trusty gauge usually predicts US economic downturns but there are some positive signs"},"alternativeTitles":{"contentPackageTitle":null,"promotionalTitle":"Investor nervousness rises as yield curve flattens"},"body":"\u003cbody\u003e\u003ccontent data-embedded=\"true\" id=\"ff43c2b2-5a4f-11e7-2b35-7545c1789969\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/ImageSet\"\u003e\u003c/content\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe leading market indicator of economic distress is flashing orange again, challenging the US Federal Reserve’s insistence on \u003ccontent id=\"19078a18-5144-11e7-bfb8-997009366969\" title=\"Investors wary of Fed’s newfound assertiveness - FT\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eraising interest rates \u003c/content\u003eand the stock market’s blithe march to record highs. But could this usually trusty gauge be faulty this time?\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eInvestors are, for the most part, as poor at forecasting recessions as professional economists, often climbing higher even as the ground quakes beneath their feet. But the so-called “yield curve” has predicted almost every US economic downturn since the second world war and is now blinking an ominous colour.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe yield curve is the slope shaped by the yields of government bonds of various maturities. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eTypically it costs more to issue long-term bonds than shorter term ones, so the curve slopes upwards most of the time. But when the yield curve flattens and eventually inverts, it tends to presage a recession. And the \u003ccontent id=\"0bdbe45c-d0fc-11e5-831d-09f7778e7377\" title=\"Yield curve recession indicator sends warning on US economy - FT\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eUS yield curve\u003c/content\u003e has dramatically flattened this year, sparking concern among some investors.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e“It’s been a pretty powerful indicator in the past,” said Gregory Peters, senior investment officer at PGIM Fixed Income. “It’s still a very instructive gauge and you have to pay attention to it.” \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe yield curve initially steepened heavily in the wake of last year’s presidential election, as investors grew more hopeful that President Donald Trump’s promised economic policies could improve economic growth and lift inflation. But, for most of 2017, it has deflated like a punctured balloon.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe widely watched spread between two and 30-year Treasury yields — one of the best measures of the yield curve — fell to a 10-year low of just 137 basis points this week, down from more than 200bp towards the end of last year. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ccontent data-embedded=\"true\" id=\"69d9cca6-5812-11e7-1ed0-0c60966b289b\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/ImageSet\"\u003e\u003c/content\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe difference between two and 10-year Treasury yields, another popular measure, has fallen to 80bp, close to the nine-year low of 75bp touched last summer.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe yield curve has been flattening as a result of the Federal Reserve raising interest rates, which pushes up the policy-sensitive two-year Treasury yield. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMeanwhile, longer term government bond yields have fallen, reflecting ebbing expectations for growth and inflation, with the latter reinforced of late by weakening oil prices. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe \u003ccontent id=\"8bb044d0-ff76-11e6-8d8e-a5e3738f9ae4\" title=\"Bond investors send warning for record high equity market - FT\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eeconomic pessimism\u003c/content\u003e signalled by long-term Treasury yields stands in sharp contrast to the ebullience of the US stock market, which hit a record high this week. Some strategists say this divergence cannot last. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003crelated id=\"8bb044d0-ff76-11e6-8d8e-a5e3738f9ae4\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003e\u003ctitle\u003eInvesting under Trump\u003c/title\u003e\u003cheadline\u003eBond investors send warning for record high equity market\n\u003c/headline\u003e\u003cmedia\u003e\u003ccontent data-embedded=\"true\" id=\"0e5b9930-0007-11e7-13e8-3279a9fb31ad\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/ImageSet\"\u003e\u003c/content\u003e\u003c/media\u003e\u003cintro\u003e\u003cp\u003eStructural reasons that have kept yields low seen staying in place\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/intro\u003e\u003c/related\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBank of America analysts recently estimated that Treasury yields either had to rise 50-65 basis points or stocks fall 13-20 per cent to reconcile the differing growth outlooks baked into their prices. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThey think a bond sell-off is the most likely outcome but fixed income investors tend to have a better grasp of economic fundamentals than equity investors.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eNonetheless, while there are reasons to be nervous about what the yield curve is whispering, many investors point out that there are also grounds for optimism. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eLonger term bond yields are also pressured by global flows of money that the Fed can do little about and which have little to do with the US economic outlook. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eChina has resumed buying Treasuries and the European Central Bank and Bank of Japan still have their foot to the monetary pedal. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThis keeps Japanese and eurozone bond yields near record lows and spurs many investors, desperate for some yield in still-safe bonds, into the US government market, muffling any signal the yield curve sends. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e“We still have these yield refugees from Japan and the eurozone,” said Jim Leaviss, head of retail fixed income at M\u0026amp;G Investments. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eHe argues that the flattening of the yield curve might even be an encouraging development for the Fed. This reflects how wary the central bank’s policymakers are of upsetting the bond market after the turbulence that followed the tapering of its quantitative easing programme, which caused mortgage costs — which are benchmarked to Treasuries — to shoot higher and \u003ca href=\"https://www.stlouisfed.org/publications/housing-market-perspectives/issue-4-march-2017/could-housing-markets-face-another-taper-tantrum-moment\" title=\"Could Housing Markets Face Another \u0026quot;Taper Tantrum\u0026quot; Moment? - St Louis Fed\"\u003ethreatened to derail \u003c/a\u003ethe housing recovery.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e“If I was the Fed, I’d be happy with this rather than unhappy,” Mr Leaviss said. “US mortgage rates are tied to the long end of the bond market and getting interest rates back to normal without startling the long end is positive.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eLarry Hatheway, chief economist and fund manager at GAM, an asset manager, points out that the yield curve is still far from inverting and argues that the recent flattening has been primarily driven by falling inflation and expectations of inflation. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eSo-called “break-even” rates — a measure of investors’ inflation expectations derived from comparing the yields of inflation-protected and conventional government bonds — have slid precipitously this year. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ccontent data-embedded=\"true\" id=\"6c8bf4ba-5812-11e7-1ed0-0c60966b289b\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/ImageSet\"\u003e\u003c/content\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhile there is clearly “nervousness” in markets about the outlook, the decline in inflation expectations has been a bigger factor in the yield curve deflation, Mr Hatheway argues. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e“There’s been a pretty significant adjustment in break-evens,” he said. “If people were really ratcheting back growth expectations, we should have seen a wider manifestation of that in markets.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eStill, those nerves are not going away — and some investors are taking money off the table as a result. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eTake Phase Capital, a computer-driven hedge fund that uses a wide variety of market indicators for its models that calculate how much risk it should take. The flattening yield curve has led it to roughly halve its market exposure, said Michael DePalma, Phase Capital chief executive. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e“We have found the yield curve to be a persistently reliable indicator,” he said. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ccontent data-embedded=\"true\" id=\"38f3c9d6-72a8-4b43-a646-6e2bfc016d6b\" {"uuid":"1c275378-55da-11e7-80b6-9bfa4c1f83d2","content":{"accessLevel":"subscribed","alternativeStandfirsts":{"promotionalStandfirst":"Peter Mandelson and John Redwood discuss the potential shape of an EU-UK deal"},"alternativeTitles":{"contentPackageTitle":null,"promotionalTitle":"One year on, how do we bridge the Brexit divide?"},"body":"\u003cbody\u003e\u003ccontent data-embedded=\"true\" id=\"70f12e74-576f-11e7-1ed0-0c60966b289b\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/ImageSet\"\u003e\u003c/content\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003ePeter Mandelson\u003c/strong\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eOf all the questions posed by the UK government’s evaporated parliamentary majority, the most important is what British voters now want from \u003ca href=\"https://www.ft.com/brexit\"\u003eBrexit\u003c/a\u003e. One ‘softer’ than set out in Theresa May’s Lancaster House speech in January? Almost certainly, as \u003ca href=\"http://www.dailymail.co.uk/news/article-4614492/Wounded-knees-PM-faces-civil-war.html\"\u003epolls\u003c/a\u003e now show. But what does that mean? And how do we work to turn ill-defined talk of \u003ccontent id=\"eebff700-5444-11e7-9fed-c19e2700005f\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003esoftness\u003c/content\u003e into something concrete and politically acceptable both in Britain and Europe.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe first softening that has to happen straightaway concerns posture: clear acceptance that no deal would be a catastrophic failure rather than an assertion of UK confidence and machismo; a constructive tone on money and \u003ccontent id=\"e86d81f8-5712-11e7-80b6-9bfa4c1f83d2\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eEU citizens’ rights \u003c/content\u003ethat understands why a fair deal on these things matters as much to the union as it does to the UK; no more bluster about regulatory competition between us and the continent. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eSo much will be built on trust between the two sides, this tone cannot be more important. Flirting with unsustainable tough talk for domestic consumption will backfire — they read our newspapers. But the real issue is how to put fresh focus on an agreement that respects the will of UK voters while mitigating the job losses and sacrifice in living standards. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003ccontent id=\"a6b9c062-dca8-11e6-86ac-f253db7791c6\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eMrs May \u003c/content\u003ecalled for a deep and comprehensive free-trade agreement. \nBut the UK prime minister is not going to get it with her existing red lines, especially for regulated services. It would have to include a willingness by the UK to consider forms of regulatory alignment and enforcement with the EU, for example in finance and broadcasting, to maintain our privileged market access. Tying our standards to EU ones in return for market access is the essence of an intelligent debate about where regulatory autonomy and access to our largest market need to be balanced.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe government should put renewed emphasis on the hints in the \u003ca href=\"https://www.gov.uk/government/speeches/the-governments-negotiating-objectives-for-exiting-the-eu-pm-speech\" title=\"www.gov.uk\"\u003eLancaster House speech\u003c/a\u003e that the UK wants to remain closely aligned with the EU in research, aviation policy and security. We should include \u003ca href=\"https://www.ft.com/stream/3cf1fba7-105b-3766-a23a-466154169657\"\u003eEuratom\u003c/a\u003e in this. We should be clear that we see ourselves caucusing with the EU on everything from climate change and multi-\u003cbr/\u003elateralism in trade policy to global financial standards. We should maintain unified capital markets, even if that means binding the UK into close supervisory co-operation with the EU. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe government should proactively argue that taking back control of migration policy does not mean closing the door to visa free travel or ending an open approach to European citizens who want to work in the UK where the labour market needs them. Rejecting the principle of freedom of movement has to give way to a discussion about how it would work in practice.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ccontent data-embedded=\"true\" id=\"a8a4e800-5748-11e7-1ed0-0c60966b289b\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/ImageSet\"\u003e\u003c/content\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe prime minister needs to deliver a Lancaster House Mark 2. Staying in the customs union is important, although originally ruled out in January in order to signal new UK autonomy in trade policy. There is no point in pretending this does not involve compromising the UK’s ability to set its own external tariffs — and by implication its ability to negotiate independent trade deals on goods. But meaningful trade deals of this kind will be hard to achieve and are, in any case, only one part of what an autonomous trade policy means. It would also mean compromise in aligning with EU product standards and in a common approach to industrial subsidies.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBut these compromises need to be weighed against the benefits: eliminating tariffs with our largest market; no origin requirements for goods and components. Even aligning with EU standards has the upside of creating a level playing field in which UK firms can rely on mutual recognition in the single market. Staying in the customs union, or, to a greater extent in the single market via something like EEA membership, would mean losing some control over EU rules but retaining all the associated trade benefits of the single market. There is no simple answer here. But there is a necessary debate.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ccontent data-embedded=\"true\" id=\"b7569fd8-97f5-479e-8fa7-585d95ed49a5\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Video\"\u003e\u003c/content\u003e\n\u003cbr/\u003e\n\n\u003cp\u003eFinally, it is important to recognise that without such compromises a hard Brexit means leaving the EU without transitional arrangements and minus the clear prospect of a deep and comprehensive trade agreement. Such a cliff edge, and without the legal continuity provided by the Great Repeal Bill, would be a disaster. \n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe distinction between hard and soft Brexits has always been an elastic concept. It understates how much could be possible if the accent was put on innovative policy approaches, with a large injection of pragmatism and compromise, and a wider view of the UK’s role as an EU partner. Global Britain is all well and good. It is time to be a whole lot clearer about what a European Britain will look like in the future.    \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eThe writer is a former UK cabinet minister and EU trade commissioner\u003c/em\u003e   \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eJohn Redwood\u003c/strong\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eWhen the British people voted for \u003ca href=\"https://www.ft.com/brexit\"\u003eBrexit\u003c/a\u003e they voted to take back control. They knew exactly what they wanted. The official Leave campaign stuck to the same vision throughout. We want control of our laws, our money and our borders. When Britain leaves the EU in 20 months’ time, that is what we will have.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003ccontent id=\"0c5c74bc-151e-11e6-b197-a4af20d5575e\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eProminent members of the Leave campaign\u003c/content\u003e stated that leaving the EU meant leaving the single market and customs union too, and stopping all contributions to the bloc’s budget once out. While \u003ccontent id=\"3756dc5a-558e-11e7-9fed-c19e2700005f\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003ePhilip Hammond\u003c/content\u003e, the chancellor, is right to say that people did not vote to be worse off, those of us who voted Leave wish the UK to agree good terms with its neighbours and friends in the EU for a positive and mutually beneficial relationship.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThis should include tariff-free trade with no new harmful barriers, continuing co-operation on security and intelligence, mutual defence through Nato, and many investment, academic and cultural links. The UK offers a lot and is happy to carry on with these contributions to the greater good. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eOnce out of the EU, Britain will regain its full voice and vote on international standards and trade bodies, giving it more of a say. Of course, the UK will no longer have a seat at the table when the EU interprets the decisions taken by global institutions for its internal market. British exporters will need to comply with EU rules when selling into the single market, just as today they have to accept US rules when selling into the American market. Once out of the EU, Britain will have two advantages: it can help shape the global standards that lie behind the EU ones, and it will not need to apply the EU variants to domestic and non-EU trade, as we do at the moment, if that is unhelpful to business.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eSome say there is no way the EU will allow tariff-free trade after Britain leaves. If the UK has to go over to World Trade Organization terms because there is no special trade deal, the EU will lose out. The UK does not sell much that would be subject to high tariffs, whereas EU farm exports would be badly hit. \n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ccontent data-embedded=\"true\" id=\"ba6708ac-5748-11e7-1ed0-0c60966b289b\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/ImageSet\"\u003e\u003c/content\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eOne of the main problems with Britain’s membership of the EU has been the continuing and huge balance of trade deficit we have run with the rest of the bloc, £70bn last year. Given the fact that we run a \u003ca href=\"http://researchbriefings.parliament.uk/ResearchBriefing/Summary/CBP-7851\"\u003esurplus with the rest of the world\u003c/a\u003e, it implies there is something about EU rules and trade arrangements that are unhelpful to us. Just look at the way the common agricultural policy has damaged British farming and turned the UK into a massive importer of EU food. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eOr look at the way the common fisheries policy has turned a nation with one of the richest fishing grounds in the world into a heavy net importer of fish. Out of the EU Britain can have a fishing policy that is kinder to our fish and fishermen. If the EU decides on high tariffs for food, the impact on prices can be cushioned by removing tariffs from agricultural products we cannot grow for ourselves from elsewhere in the world.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThere is also the problem of too many people coming to Britain from the EU to look for low-paid jobs. We need to do better at training and educating people already settled in the UK to do the jobs that need doing, and to help business raise the skill and pay levels as we do so. Britain has always made clear that a new migration policy will welcome anyone with the skills and educational qualifications we seek, and anyone coming in on a company transfer who is needed. \n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThere is no “cliff edge” ahead. The next 20 months should be used by both sides in the Brexit negotiations to ensure exports run smoothly after we leave. Some say, for example, that planes will not fly to Paris from London for want of an air services agreement on the day Britain departs the EU. But many countries around the world lack such agreements with others, yet their planes fly with landing rights granted. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe UK can agree landing rights even if the EU fails to agree a full air services agreement with us. There is plenty of time to make the customs documentation work for the new relationship. If all else fails and we are left with WTO rules, then we know how that works as that is what drives all our non-EU trade today.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBusiness should therefore get behind the government negotiators who want to sort out these matters in a timely way. It should understand that Britain can be better off out, as it will at last draw up policies for deeply damaged sectors like fishing and farming where we currently import too much thanks to EU rules. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe EU is bound by article 21 of its own\u003ca href=\"http://www.lisbon-treaty.org/wcm/the-lisbon-treaty/treaty-on-european-union-and-comments/title-5-general-provisions-on-the-unions-external-action-and-specific-provisions/chapter-1-general-provisions-on-the-unions-external-action/101-article-10a.html\"\u003e treaty\u003c/a\u003e to have positive and friendly relationships with neighbouring states based on the pursuit of trade and prosperity. Obeying that makes sense for the EU and for Britain.    \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eThe writer is Conservative member of parliament for Wokingham\u003c/em\u003e   \u003c/p\u003e\n\n\u003ch3 class=\"ft-subhead\"\u003eLetter in response to these articles:\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u003ccontent id=\"c2ff3136-583f-11e7-9fed-c19e2700005f\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eThis sorry situation may force us to change / From Chris Preston, Cowes, Isle of Wight,
