{"uuid":"1c275378-55da-11e7-80b6-9bfa4c1f83d2","content":{"accessLevel":"subscribed","alternativeStandfirsts":{"promotionalStandfirst":"Peter Mandelson and John Redwood discuss the potential shape of an EU-UK deal"},"alternativeTitles":{"contentPackageTitle":null,"promotionalTitle":"One year on, how do we bridge the Brexit divide?"},"body":"\u003cbody\u003e\u003ccontent data-embedded=\"true\" id=\"70f12e74-576f-11e7-1ed0-0c60966b289b\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/ImageSet\"\u003e\u003c/content\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003ePeter Mandelson\u003c/strong\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eOf all the questions posed by the UK government’s evaporated parliamentary majority, the most important is what British voters now want from \u003ca href=\"https://www.ft.com/brexit\"\u003eBrexit\u003c/a\u003e. One ‘softer’ than set out in Theresa May’s Lancaster House speech in January? Almost certainly, as \u003ca href=\"http://www.dailymail.co.uk/news/article-4614492/Wounded-knees-PM-faces-civil-war.html\"\u003epolls\u003c/a\u003e now show. But what does that mean? And how do we work to turn ill-defined talk of \u003ccontent id=\"eebff700-5444-11e7-9fed-c19e2700005f\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003esoftness\u003c/content\u003e into something concrete and politically acceptable both in Britain and Europe.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe first softening that has to happen straightaway concerns posture: clear acceptance that no deal would be a catastrophic failure rather than an assertion of UK confidence and machismo; a constructive tone on money and \u003ccontent id=\"e86d81f8-5712-11e7-80b6-9bfa4c1f83d2\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eEU citizens’ rights \u003c/content\u003ethat understands why a fair deal on these things matters as much to the union as it does to the UK; no more bluster about regulatory competition between us and the continent. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eSo much will be built on trust between the two sides, this tone cannot be more important. Flirting with unsustainable tough talk for domestic consumption will backfire — they read our newspapers. But the real issue is how to put fresh focus on an agreement that respects the will of UK voters while mitigating the job losses and sacrifice in living standards. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\n\u003ccontent id=\"a6b9c062-dca8-11e6-86ac-f253db7791c6\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eMrs May \u003c/content\u003ecalled for a deep and comprehensive free-trade agreement. \nBut the UK prime minister is not going to get it with her existing red lines, especially for regulated services. It would have to include a willingness by the UK to consider forms of regulatory alignment and enforcement with the EU, for example in finance and broadcasting, to maintain our privileged market access. Tying our standards to EU ones in return for market access is the essence of an intelligent debate about where regulatory autonomy and access to our largest market need to be balanced.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe government should put renewed emphasis on the hints in the \u003ca href=\"https://www.gov.uk/government/speeches/the-governments-negotiating-objectives-for-exiting-the-eu-pm-speech\" title=\"www.gov.uk\"\u003eLancaster House speech\u003c/a\u003e that the UK wants to remain closely aligned with the EU in research, aviation policy and security. We should include \u003ca href=\"https://www.ft.com/stream/3cf1fba7-105b-3766-a23a-466154169657\"\u003eEuratom\u003c/a\u003e in this. We should be clear that we see ourselves caucusing with the EU on everything from climate change and multi-\u003cbr/\u003elateralism in trade policy to global financial standards. We should maintain unified capital markets, even if that means binding the UK into close supervisory co-operation with the EU. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe government should proactively argue that taking back control of migration policy does not mean closing the door to visa free travel or ending an open approach to European citizens who want to work in the UK where the labour market needs them. Rejecting the principle of freedom of movement has to give way to a discussion about how it would work in practice.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ccontent data-embedded=\"true\" id=\"a8a4e800-5748-11e7-1ed0-0c60966b289b\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/ImageSet\"\u003e\u003c/content\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe prime minister needs to deliver a Lancaster House Mark 2. Staying in the customs union is important, although originally ruled out in January in order to signal new UK autonomy in trade policy. There is no point in pretending this does not involve compromising the UK’s ability to set its own external tariffs — and by implication its ability to negotiate independent trade deals on goods. But meaningful trade deals of this kind will be hard to achieve and are, in any case, only one part of what an autonomous trade policy means. It would also mean compromise in aligning with EU product standards and in a common approach to industrial subsidies.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBut these compromises need to be weighed against the benefits: eliminating tariffs with our largest market; no origin requirements for goods and components. Even aligning with EU standards has the upside of creating a level playing field in which UK firms can rely on mutual recognition in the single market. Staying in the customs union, or, to a greater extent in the single market via something like EEA membership, would mean losing some control over EU rules but retaining all the associated trade benefits of the single market. There is no simple answer here. But there is a necessary debate.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ccontent data-embedded=\"true\" id=\"b7569fd8-97f5-479e-8fa7-585d95ed49a5\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Video\"\u003e\u003c/content\u003e\n\u003cbr/\u003e\n\n\u003cp\u003eFinally, it is important to recognise that without such compromises a hard Brexit means leaving the EU without transitional arrangements and minus the clear prospect of a deep and comprehensive trade agreement. Such a cliff edge, and without the legal continuity provided by the Great Repeal Bill, would be a disaster. \n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe distinction between hard and soft Brexits has always been an elastic concept. It understates how much could be possible if the accent was put on innovative policy approaches, with a large injection of pragmatism and compromise, and a wider view of the UK’s role as an EU partner. Global Britain is all well and good. It is time to be a whole lot clearer about what a European Britain will look like in the future.    \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eThe writer is a former UK cabinet minister and EU trade commissioner\u003c/em\u003e   \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eJohn Redwood\u003c/strong\u003e\n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eWhen the British people voted for \u003ca href=\"https://www.ft.com/brexit\"\u003eBrexit\u003c/a\u003e they voted to take back control. They knew exactly what they wanted. The official Leave campaign stuck to the same vision throughout. We want control of our laws, our money and our borders. When Britain leaves the EU in 20 months’ time, that is what we will have.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003ccontent id=\"0c5c74bc-151e-11e6-b197-a4af20d5575e\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eProminent members of the Leave campaign\u003c/content\u003e stated that leaving the EU meant leaving the single market and customs union too, and stopping all contributions to the bloc’s budget once out. While \u003ccontent id=\"3756dc5a-558e-11e7-9fed-c19e2700005f\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003ePhilip Hammond\u003c/content\u003e, the chancellor, is right to say that people did not vote to be worse off, those of us who voted Leave wish the UK to agree good terms with its neighbours and friends in the EU for a positive and mutually beneficial relationship.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThis should include tariff-free trade with no new harmful barriers, continuing co-operation on security and intelligence, mutual defence through Nato, and many investment, academic and cultural links. The UK offers a lot and is happy to carry on with these contributions to the greater good. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eOnce out of the EU, Britain will regain its full voice and vote on international standards and trade bodies, giving it more of a say. Of course, the UK will no longer have a seat at the table when the EU interprets the decisions taken by global institutions for its internal market. British exporters will need to comply with EU rules when selling into the single market, just as today they have to accept US rules when selling into the American market. Once out of the EU, Britain will have two advantages: it can help shape the global standards that lie behind the EU ones, and it will not need to apply the EU variants to domestic and non-EU trade, as we do at the moment, if that is unhelpful to business.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eSome say there is no way the EU will allow tariff-free trade after Britain leaves. If the UK has to go over to World Trade Organization terms because there is no special trade deal, the EU will lose out. The UK does not sell much that would be subject to high tariffs, whereas EU farm exports would be badly hit. \n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ccontent data-embedded=\"true\" id=\"ba6708ac-5748-11e7-1ed0-0c60966b289b\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/ImageSet\"\u003e\u003c/content\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eOne of the main problems with Britain’s membership of the EU has been the continuing and huge balance of trade deficit we have run with the rest of the bloc, £70bn last year. Given the fact that we run a \u003ca href=\"http://researchbriefings.parliament.uk/ResearchBriefing/Summary/CBP-7851\"\u003esurplus with the rest of the world\u003c/a\u003e, it implies there is something about EU rules and trade arrangements that are unhelpful to us. Just look at the way the common agricultural policy has damaged British farming and turned the UK into a massive importer of EU food. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eOr look at the way the common fisheries policy has turned a nation with one of the richest fishing grounds in the world into a heavy net importer of fish. Out of the EU Britain can have a fishing policy that is kinder to our fish and fishermen. If the EU decides on high tariffs for food, the impact on prices can be cushioned by removing tariffs from agricultural products we cannot grow for ourselves from elsewhere in the world.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThere is also the problem of too many people coming to Britain from the EU to look for low-paid jobs. We need to do better at training and educating people already settled in the UK to do the jobs that need doing, and to help business raise the skill and pay levels as we do so. Britain has always made clear that a new migration policy will welcome anyone with the skills and educational qualifications we seek, and anyone coming in on a company transfer who is needed. \n\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThere is no “cliff edge” ahead. The next 20 months should be used by both sides in the Brexit negotiations to ensure exports run smoothly after we leave. Some say, for example, that planes will not fly to Paris from London for want of an air services agreement on the day Britain departs the EU. But many countries around the world lack such agreements with others, yet their planes fly with landing rights granted. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe UK can agree landing rights even if the EU fails to agree a full air services agreement with us. There is plenty of time to make the customs documentation work for the new relationship. If all else fails and we are left with WTO rules, then we know how that works as that is what drives all our non-EU trade today.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBusiness should therefore get behind the government negotiators who want to sort out these matters in a timely way. It should understand that Britain can be better off out, as it will at last draw up policies for deeply damaged sectors like fishing and farming where we currently import too much thanks to EU rules. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe EU is bound by article 21 of its own\u003ca href=\"http://www.lisbon-treaty.org/wcm/the-lisbon-treaty/treaty-on-european-union-and-comments/title-5-general-provisions-on-the-unions-external-action-and-specific-provisions/chapter-1-general-provisions-on-the-unions-external-action/101-article-10a.html\"\u003e treaty\u003c/a\u003e to have positive and friendly relationships with neighbouring states based on the pursuit of trade and prosperity. Obeying that makes sense for the EU and for Britain.    \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eThe writer is Conservative member of parliament for Wokingham\u003c/em\u003e   \u003c/p\u003e\n\n\u003ch3 class=\"ft-subhead\"\u003eLetter in response to these articles:\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u003ccontent id=\"c2ff3136-583f-11e7-9fed-c19e2700005f\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003eThis sorry situation may force us to change / From Chris Preston, Cowes, Isle of Wight, {"uuid":"502af52e-5a8c-11e7-b553-e2df1b0c3220","content":{"accessLevel":"premium","alternativeStandfirsts":{"promotionalStandfirst":"The sage’s punt on Store suggests opportunity in property for careful investors"},"alternativeTitles":{"contentPackageTitle":null,"promotionalTitle":"Buffett/US property: good for the gander"},"body":"\u003cbody\u003e\u003ccontent data-embedded=\"true\" id=\"8ebbf04c-5710-11e7-1ed0-0c60966b289b\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/ImageSet\"\u003e\u003c/content\u003e\u003cp\u003eGyms, cinemas, day care centres. Warren Buffett is betting that Americans will step away from their sofas and venture into the real world. On Monday an affiliate of Mr Buffett’s Berkshire Hathaway took a 10 per cent stake, costing less than $400m, in an obscure US property owner, Store Capital. The real estate investment trust (Reit) owns freestanding properties that lease space to companies specialising in “experiences” — AMC theatres, Cadence Education, O’Charley’s steakhouses.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eEven a limited exposure to US retail has cast a pall over property owners such as Store, whose shares have fallen 30 per cent in the past year. Mr Buffett must believe that whatever the onslaught of Amazon, there are opportunities for careful investors.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFor many merchants, owning property is not worth the trouble. Reits such as Store continuously acquire property — often through sale-and-leaseback transactions with merchants — and become landlords. They charge modestly escalating rents and their \u003ccontent id=\"d0aa189e-7aed-11e6-ae24-f193b105145e\" title=\"Real estate gains independence on US stock market\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003etax-efficient structure \u003c/content\u003emeans that by paying virtually all their profits as dividends they are spared corporate-level taxes. With dividend yields greater than 5 per cent, Reits have prospered in a yield-starved world. Investors just have to hope they have a canny understanding of tenant risk. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eWhile 80 per cent of Store’s revenue comes from outside retail, one customer, camping equipment seller Gander Mountain, recently filed for bankruptcy. The tenant only accounts for 2 per cent of Store’s rents collected, however. Store properties on average generate cash flow twice the size of the rent expense it charges indicating a significant cushion.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eA leverage-fuelled acquisition strategy brings its own risk. Store’s debt/ebitda ratio of 6 times is juicy. The risk is less about changing consumer habits. Mr Buffett should worry when the whole economy broadly turns down and consumers have less to spend from both their living rooms and the outdoors.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eEmail the {"uuid":"ff988376-7909-3bdd-a6a3-88d3d468497e","content":{"accessLevel":"subscribed","body":"\u003cbody\u003e\u003cp\u003eIt’s time to call a spade a spade. Or a duffle a coat. Or something. As New York fashion week kicks off fashion month on Thursday (joy! rapture!) I think it’s about time we face up to certain realities of the form that, for whatever reason, we have seemed unwilling to acknowledge in the past. Starting with …\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFact 1: Designers never want to show in a shared venue.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAll that breast-beating that has gone on this season about the \u003ca href=\"http://www.nydailynews.com/life-style/fashion/lincoln-center-fashion-designers-article-1.1565692\"\u003emigration away from Lincoln Center\u003c/a\u003e should not have surprised anyone. It was bound to happen – and it has happened everywhere. Anyone remember the Fiera in Milan, aka the Italian answer to the tents? Abandoned by brands. How about the much-ballyhooed venues in the Carrousel du Louvre in Paris? Ditto. It has been turned into a place for trade shows. As Narciso Rodriguez said when I discussed this with him, “I spend a lot of time trying to figure out my specific point of view, so it doesn’t really make sense for me to then show the collection in the same place as a bunch of other designers. It sort of defeats the purpose.” It’s the one reliable thing: like death and taxes.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003ca href=\"http://www.wwd.com/fashion-news/designer-luxury/alexander-wang-moving-show-to-brooklyn-7361146\"\u003eAlexander Wang’s show\u003c/a\u003e on Saturday in Brooklyn is widely being held up as the ultimate example of this trend (OMG! An outer borough!), but it happened by accident – his usual venue was unavailable and he had to scramble to get another – and while they are putting the best spin on the situation (he’s a pioneer!) my guess is next season they will be back in Manhattan. Though not back in the tents.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIndeed, students of history will thus know that fact is, no matter what happens with the next Great Shared Venue, \u003ca href=\"http://www.elle.com/news/fashion-style/nyfw-hudson-yards-move\"\u003eHudson Yards\u003c/a\u003e, at a certain point, designers are going to leave. Let’s just admit it, and spend our tax money in more productive ways.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFact 2: Every virtual brand wants a physical product\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eJust as e-brands \u003ca href=\"http://blogs.ft.com/material-world/2012/11/30/in-fashion-digital-is-not-enough/\"\u003eBonobos, Warby Parker\u003c/a\u003e and Moda Operandi now have bricks and mortar “showrooms” (a store by another name) so customers can reach out and touch their products, so, too, e-ditorial outlets are turning to actual paper magazines. Today Net a Porter is finally launching their print product, \u003ca href=\"http://www.businessoffashion.com/2014/02/porter-magazine-net-a-porter.html\"\u003ePorter\u003c/a\u003e, with rumours that they may follow with a men’s title, and on Friday Editorialist, the New York-based luxury accessory and jewellery ecommerce/ezine, launches their print incarnation. I’m not entirely convinced it’s good business (though I do think when it comes to advertising in magazines, luxury brands like nice paper more than the web; the pictures just look better), but I do think it’s human nature: people want to hold what they make in their hands, otherwise it doesn’t feel real. So I expect more of it to happen, couched under the rubric Omni-channel.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFact 3: Weather has no effect on what people wear because of street style\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAfter seasons of covering NYFW in February, through all sorts of inclement weather, I have learnt that temporal reality has nothing to do with fashion footwear. Hence I expect the coming weekend snowstorm that has designers showing on Sunday biting their nails will have no effect at all on the shoes we see. This is being driven, as usual, by street style photographers: after all, how much better are your chances of being snapped if you march through a snowdrift in stilettos than in moon boots?\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAnd we wonder why the world thinks fashion people are nuts. Happy fashion week.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003ePS: for shots and schtick from the front lines, follow us on \u003ca href=\"https://twitter.com/FTLuxury360\"\u003e@FTLuxury360\u003c/a\u003e, \u003ca href=\"https://twitter.com/VVFriedman\"\u003e@VVFriedman\u003c/a\u003e, \u003ca href=\"https://twitter.com/LizziePaton\"\u003e@lizziepaton\u003c/a\u003e, or #FTLuxury. I promise to take pictures of the shoes.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/body\u003e","brands":[{"id":"http://api.ft.com/things/13006c72-7d1b-47a0-96fe-d1ad1f12de9f"},{"id":"http://api.ft.com/things/dbb0bdae-1f0c-11e4-b0cb-b2227cce2b54"}],"byline":"Vanessa Friedman","canBeDistributed":"yes","canBeSyndicated":"verify","comments":{"enabled":true},"description":null,"externalBinaryUrl":null,"firstPublishedDate":"2014-02-06T12:08:28.000Z","identifiers":[{"authority":"http://api.ft.com/system/FT-LABS-WP-1-106","identifierValue":"http://blogs.ft.com/material-world/2014/02/06/hard-truths-this-new-york-fashion-week/"},{"authority":"http://api.ft.com/system/FT-LABS-WP-1-106","identifierValue":"http://blogs.ft.com/material-world/?p=84412"}],"internalBinaryUrl":null,"lastModified":"2018-04-04T12:59:07.031Z","mainImage":null,"mediaType":null,"members":null,"opening":"\u003cbody\u003e\u003cp\u003eIt’s time to call a spade a spade. {"uuid":"502af52e-5a8c-11e7-b553-e2df1b0c3220","content":{"accessLevel":"premium","alternativeStandfirsts":{"promotionalStandfirst":"The sage’s punt on Store suggests opportunity in property for careful investors"},"alternativeTitles":{"contentPackageTitle":null,"promotionalTitle":"Buffett/US property: good for the gander"},"body":"\u003cbody\u003e\u003ccontent data-embedded=\"true\" id=\"8ebbf04c-5710-11e7-1ed0-0c60966b289b\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/ImageSet\"\u003e\u003c/content\u003e\u003cp\u003eGyms, cinemas, day care centres. Warren Buffett is betting that Americans will step away from their sofas and venture into the real world. On Monday an affiliate of Mr Buffett’s Berkshire Hathaway took a 10 per cent stake, costing less than $400m, in an obscure US property owner, Store Capital. The real estate investment trust (Reit) owns freestanding properties that lease space to companies specialising in “experiences” — AMC theatres, Cadence Education, O’Charley’s steakhouses.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eEven a limited exposure to US retail has cast a pall over property owners such as Store, whose shares have fallen 30 per cent in the past year. Mr Buffett must believe that whatever the onslaught of Amazon, there are opportunities for careful investors.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFor many merchants, owning property is not worth the trouble. Reits such as Store continuously acquire property — often through sale-and-leaseback transactions with merchants — and become landlords. They charge modestly escalating rents and their \u003ccontent id=\"d0aa189e-7aed-11e6-ae24-f193b105145e\" title=\"Real estate gains independence on US stock market\" type=\"http://www.ft.com/ontology/content/Article\"\u003etax-efficient structure \u003c/content\u003emeans that by paying virtually all their profits as dividends they are spared corporate-level taxes. With dividend yields greater than 5 per cent, Reits have prospered in a yield-starved world. Investors just have to hope they have a canny understanding of tenant risk. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eWhile 80 per cent of Store’s revenue comes from outside retail, one customer, camping equipment seller Gander Mountain, recently filed for bankruptcy. The tenant only accounts for 2 per cent of Store’s rents collected, however. Store properties on average generate cash flow twice the size of the rent expense it charges indicating a significant cushion.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eA leverage-fuelled acquisition strategy brings its own risk. Store’s debt/ebitda ratio of 6 times is juicy. The risk is less about changing consumer habits. Mr Buffett should worry when the whole economy broadly turns down and consumers have less to spend from both their living rooms and the outdoors.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eEmail the
